2:25:1412:0:14:72:511:700:12:Times:12:Times:Disentangling the Contemporaneous and Dynamic Effects of Human and Health Capital on
2:26:1496:14:14:72:201:686:12:Times:12:Times:Wages over the Life Cycle
2:27:1521:14:14:72:333:672:12:Times:12:Times:Donna B. Gilleskie, Euna Han, and Edward C. Norton
2:28:1571:14:14:72:234:658:12:Times:12:Times:NBER Working Paper No. 22430
2:29:1599:14:14:72:119:644:12:Times:12:Times:July 2016
2:30:1608:14:28:72:170:630:12:Times:12:Times:JEL No. I12,J12,J13
2:31:1627:28:28:273:338:602:12:Times:12:Times:ABSTRACT
2:32:1635:28:14:72:546:574:12:Times:12:Times:In  this  study  we  quantify  the  life-cycle  effects  of  human  and  health  capital  on  the  wage 
2:33:1739:14:14:72:545:560:12:Times:12:Times:distribution of females, with a focus on health measured by body mass. We use NLSY79 data on 
2:34:1831:14:14:71:546:546:12:Times:12:Times:women followed annually up to twenty years during the time of their lives when average annual 
2:35:1924:14:14:72:546:532:12:Times:12:Times:weight  gain  is  greatest. We  allow  body  mass  to  explain  variation  in  wages  contemporaneously 
2:36:2027:14:14:71:546:518:12:Times:12:Times:conditional on observed measures of human capital and productivity histories (namely, education, 
2:37:2123:14:14:71:546:504:12:Times:12:Times:employment  experience,  marital  status, and  family  size)  and  dynamically  over  the  life  cycle 
2:38:2225:14:14:71:545:490:12:Times:12:Times:through  its  impact  on  the  endogenous  histories of  behaviors  that  determine  wages.  We  find 
2:39:2326:14:14:71:545:476:12:Times:12:Times:significant  differences  in  the  contemporaneous  effect  and the  dynamic  effect  of  body  mass  on 
2:40:2430:14:42:71:457:462:12:Times:12:Times:wages, both across females of different races and over the distribution of wages.
2:41:2510:42:14:72:164:420:12:Times:12:Times:Donna B. Gilleskie
2:42:2528:14:14:72:198:406:12:Times:12:Times:Department of Economics
2:43:2551:14:14:72:273:392:12:Times:12:Times:University of North Carolina, Chapel Hill
2:44:2592:14:14:72:207:378:12:Times:12:Times:CB #3305, 6B Gardner Hall
2:45:2617:14:14:72:211:364:12:Times:12:Times:Chapel Hill, NC 27599-3305
2:46:2643:14:28:72:197:350:12:Times:12:Times:donna_gilleskie@unc.edu

2:50:2714:14:14:72:160:280:12:Times:12:Times:Yonsei University

3:64:3008:0:34:72:208:708:17:g_d0_f2:17:g_d0_f2:1  Introduction
3:65:3023:34:22:72:540:674:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:It is common for economists to evaluate the contemporaneous effects of policy and behavioral
3:66:3115:22:22:72:540:652:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:variables  on  human  capital  outcomes  such  as  education,  employment,  and  wages.   With
3:67:3209:22:22:72:540:630:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:increasing  interest  lately,  researchers  are  studying  the  effect  of  early  life  (and  in  utero)
3:68:3314:22:22:72:540:608:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:exposures on these later life outcomes (Heckman, et al., 2013).  However, what is lacking in
3:69:3406:22:22:72:540:586:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:the literature is a dynamic analysis of life-cycle impacts of evolving variables on time-varying
3:70:3502:22:22:72:540:564:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:outcomes.  For example, economists may examine the impact of early life health (e.g., birth
3:71:3593:22:22:72:540:542:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:weight, childhood illness, mother’s smoking behavior while pregnant) on later life outcomes
3:72:3684:22:22:72:540:520:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:(e.g.,  adult  weight,  human  capital  accumulation,  mortality)  but,  generally,  are  unable  to
3:73:3784:22:21:72:540:498:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:demonstrate  the  mechanisms  through  which  variation  in  early  life  health  affects  later  life
3:74:3886:21:22:72:540:477:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:outcomes.  This omission often stems from lack of data on individuals at multiple times in
3:75:3976:22:22:72:540:455:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:their lives over a long period.  In addition to the fact that the outcome of interest may vary
3:76:4070:22:22:72:540:433:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:over the life cycle,  the explanatory variable itself (e.g.,  health) may evolve over time as a
3:77:4165:22:22:72:540:411:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:dynamic process.  Hence,  as initial health impacts subsequent health,  time-varying health
3:78:4256:22:22:72:540:389:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:may  impact  the  outcome  of  interest  over  time.   Additionally,  causality  may  run  in  both
3:79:4355:22:22:72:540:367:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:directions:  for example,  health impacts employment and employment impacts subsequent
3:80:4441:22:22:72:540:345:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:health.1Thus, it is important to model the endogeneity of both time-varying variables and
3:81:4530:22:22:72:352:323:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:to capture the dynamic impacts of each on each other.
3:82:4583:22:22:100:540:301:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:In this study we quantify the life-cycle effects of human and health capital on wages
3:83:4668:22:22:72:540:279:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:of females, with a focus on health measured by body mass.  We use data on women followed
3:84:4756:22:22:72:540:257:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:annually up to twenty years during the time of their lives when average annual weight gain
3:85:4846:22:21:72:540:235:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:is  greatest.   We  allow  body  mass  to  explain  variation  in  wages  contemporaneously  condi-
3:86:4945:21:22:72:540:214:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:tional on observed measures of human capital and productivity histories (namely, education,
3:87:5036:22:22:72:540:192:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:employment experience, marital status, and family size) and dynamically over the life cycle
3:88:5127:22:22:72:495:170:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:through its impact on the endogenous histories of behaviors that determine wages.
3:89:5208:22:22:100:540:148:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:It has been shown that the body mass of females is negatively correlated with wages
3:90:5291:22:22:72:540:126:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:(Cawley, 2004).  The evidence in much of the literature reflects the contemporaneous rela-
3:91:5381:22:18:72:540:104:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:tionship between current body mass and current wages.  Even in the cases where longitudinal
3:92:5472:18:15:85:539:86:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:1Employment  may  impact  health  directly  through  injury  or  work-related  stress  or  indirectly  through
3:93:5582:15:0:72:256:71:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:income and consumption of health inputs.
4:94:5623:0:22:72:540:708:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:observations on individuals have been available, researchers focus on the impact of current
4:95:5714:22:22:72:539:686:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:weight status on current earnings, and ignore the evolutionary process of weight gain.2While
4:96:5806:22:22:72:540:664:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:theory suggests avenues through which the history of an individual’s body mass may impact
4:97:5895:22:22:72:540:642:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:wages in the present (e.g., through educational attainment and work experience), the causal
4:98:5986:22:22:72:540:620:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:relationship between past health (body mass) and accumulation of human capital is rarely
4:99:6074:22:22:72:539:598:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:included in published analyses of weight on wages.3In our effort to understand how wages
4:100:6162:22:22:72:540:576:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:vary with a determinant of productivity such as body mass that evolves over time, we argue
4:101:6252:22:22:72:540:554:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:that  it  is  important  to  consider  the  effects  of  this  characteristic  on  other  decisions  made
4:102:6357:22:22:72:540:532:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:jointly with employment over the life cycle.  Using a long panel of observations on young
4:103:6446:22:22:72:540:510:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:women as they approach middle age, we capture the influence of an evolving body mass on
4:104:6533:22:22:72:540:488:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:life-cycle decisions pertaining to schooling, employment, marital status, and family size.  The
4:105:6628:22:21:72:540:466:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:history of these decisions, as well as one’s body mass history, define cumulative measures of
4:106:6721:21:22:72:540:445:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:human capital and other productivity measures that explain variation in observed wages.  In
4:107:6812:22:22:72:507:423:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:turn, these behaviors may also impact the evolution of body mass over the life cycle.
4:108:6897:22:22:100:540:401:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Even after accounting for the effect of an evolving body mass on the productive de-
4:109:6980:22:22:72:540:379:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:terminants of wages that accumulate over the life cycle (e.g., educational attainment, work
4:110:7071:22:22:72:540:357:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:experience, marital status, and family size), variations in contemporaneous body mass may
4:111:7160:22:22:72:540:335:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:still explain observed variation in contemporaneous wages.  A significant contemporaneous
4:112:7249:22:22:72:540:313:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:effect may reflect wage penalties associated with lower productivity (i.e., through reduced
4:113:7340:22:22:72:540:291:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:health) conditional on other observed measures of productivity.  That is, the variables that
4:114:7432:22:22:72:540:269:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:traditionally summarize one’s human capital may not fully capture productivity.  Alterna-
4:115:7521:22:22:72:540:247:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:tively, a contemporaneous effect of body mass may reflect employer preference for employees
4:116:7612:22:22:72:540:225:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:with a particular outward appearance due to concerns about high expected medical care ex-
4:117:7701:22:21:72:540:203:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:penditure  affecting  firm  health  insurance  costs  or  consumer  distaste  for  obesity.   Finally,
4:118:7803:21:22:72:540:182:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:employers  may  simply  discriminate  (either  statistically  or  based  on  own  tastes)  against
4:119:7901:22:22:72:540:160:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:women with higher body mass.  In this paper, we establish the existence (or not) of a con-
4:120:7991:22:18:72:540:138:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:temporaneous wage penalty of body mass while also measuring the dynamic impact of body
4:121:8077:18:15:85:539:120:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:2Only  a  few  papers,  to  our  knowledge,  consider  the  effects  of  current  and  past  body  mass  on  current
4:122:8193:15:12:72:540:105:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:earnings (Chen, 2012; Pinkston, 2015).  Chen includes body mass from a decade prior and Pinkston includes
4:123:8298:12:12:72:540:93:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:body mass from one year ago.  Pinkston’s use of lagged wages to explain current wages and twice lagged
4:124:8400:12:8:72:369:81:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:wages as instruments allows for indirect life-cycle body mass effects.
4:125:8470:8:16:85:539:73:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:3Han, et al.  (2011) find an indirect wage penalty of body weight in one’s late teens through its effect on
4:126:8577:16:0:72:358:57:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:education and occupation outcomes measured in the late thirties.
5:127:8642:0:22:72:540:708:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:mass on wages; we do not differentiate between individual productivity, employer preference,
5:128:8734:22:22:72:540:686:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:or employer discrimination as explanations for the contemporaneous effect.  Admittedly, they
5:129:8826:22:22:72:540:664:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:each may partially explain the current market value (i.e., wage), or marginal productivity,
5:130:8917:22:22:72:190:642:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:of an individual’s time.
5:131:8941:22:22:100:540:620:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Our contribution is the careful, quantifiable distinction between the wage effects of body
5:132:9031:22:22:72:540:598:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:mass characterized by life-cycle human capital differences (i.e., the indirect or dynamic effect)
5:133:9128:22:22:72:540:576:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:and those reflecting additional differences in current valuations of market work (i.e., the direct
5:134:9226:22:22:72:540:554:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:or  contemporaneous  effect).   To  disentangle  these  life-cycle  and  contemporaneous  effects
5:135:9323:22:22:72:540:532:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:we  use  a  multiple  equation,  dynamic  empirical  model  to  capture  the  annual  employment
5:136:9419:22:22:72:540:510:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:decisions and wage distribution of women aged 18 to 45 while jointly explaining education,
5:137:9509:22:22:72:466:488:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:marital, and family size histories, and the evolution of body mass, over time.
5:138:9587:22:21:100:540:466:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Three potential sources of bias compromise measurement of the role of health capital
5:139:9671:21:22:72:540:445:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:(i.e., body mass) in explaining wages: selection, endogeneity, and measurement error.4First,
5:140:9763:22:22:72:540:423:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:observed wages of employed individuals reflect accepted wage offers, yet we want to measure
5:141:9854:22:22:72:540:401:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:the labor market’s valuation of particular individual wage determinants unconditional on the
5:142:9946:22:22:72:540:379:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:observed employment decision.  Individuals who chose to work in the formal labor market
5:143:10033:22:22:72:540:357:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:are a self-selected group:  only those who receive wage offers above an individual reservation
5:144:10127:22:22:72:540:335:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:wage are observed to work.  Estimation using the self-selected sample will result in biased
5:145:10218:22:22:72:540:313:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:coefficients on explanatory variables for wages if non-random selection into employment is
5:146:10308:22:22:72:540:291:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:not modeled jointly with the observed wages.5Thus, we model non-, part-time, and full-time
5:147:10398:22:22:71:539:269:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:employment over the life cycle jointly with wages conditional on employment, allowing the
5:148:10487:22:22:71:539:247:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:observed employment outcome and wages to be correlated through common unobservables.
5:149:10571:22:22:71:539:225:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Second,  the  human  capital  and  other  productivity  characteristics  that  determine  pay  for
5:150:10669:22:21:71:539:203:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:work reflect life-cycle decisions of an individual.  These wage determinants include educa-
5:151:10760:21:18:71:539:182:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:tional  attainment,  work  experience,  marital  status,  number  of  children  in  household,  and
5:152:10859:18:16:85:539:164:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:4These same biases impact evaluation of human capital and other endogenous wage determinants, which
5:153:10958:16:8:72:145:148:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:we also measure.
5:154:10974:8:16:85:539:140:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:5In addition to the effects of body mass on the wage distribution, the probability of receiving an employ-
5:155:11080:16:12:72:540:124:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:ment offer may be impacted by one’s weight, thus influencing accumulation of work experience over the life
5:156:11186:12:0:72:443:112:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:cycle.  Much of the literature on weight and wages ignores this employment selection.
6:157:11272:0:22:72:540:708:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:health (measured by body mass in this paper).  Endogeneity suggests that unobserved char-
6:158:11361:22:22:72:539:686:12:g_d0_f3:8:g_d0_f4:acteristics that explain these (time-varying) decisions may also explain differences in wages.6
6:159:11456:22:22:72:540:664:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Thus,  we use a flexible,  latent factor approach to model the permanent and time-varying
6:160:11545:22:22:72:540:642:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:unobserved heterogeneity (UH) that is correlated with wages and their endogenous deter-
6:161:11632:22:22:72:540:620:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:minants.  Third, the measurement error (i.e., reporting, subjectivity, and rounding errors)
6:162:11723:22:22:72:540:598:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:that accompanies self-reported individual-level characteristics (e.g., weight and height) can
6:163:11816:22:22:72:539:576:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:lead to inconsistent estimates.7Our latent factor method that accounts for individual-level
6:164:11907:22:22:72:334:554:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:unobservables addresses potential attenuation bias.
6:165:11958:22:22:100:540:532:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:We extend the literature in several dimensions.  First, we analyze the effects of body
6:166:12044:22:22:72:540:510:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:mass  on  wages  using  yearly  observations  on  a  cohort  of  women  followed  for  twenty  con-
6:167:12143:22:22:72:540:488:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:secutive years (National Longitudinal Survey of Youth (NLSY) 1979 cohort).  Our research
6:168:12231:22:21:72:540:466:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:sample includes annual observations from the ages of 18-26 in 1983 through the ages of 37-45
6:169:12323:21:22:72:540:445:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:in 2002.  These twenty years of data on the same individuals provide a quite comprehensive
6:170:12413:22:22:72:540:423:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:picture of life changes from young adulthood to middle age.  This point of reference allows
6:171:12504:22:22:72:540:401:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:for a better understanding of both body mass and wage dynamics and provides a perspective
6:172:12593:22:22:72:540:379:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:that differs from (repeated) cross-sectional analyses.  We are able to quantify the effects of
6:173:12687:22:22:72:540:357:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:(and determinants of) body mass as an individual ages, and broaden the interpretation from
6:174:12777:22:22:72:540:335:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:one of aggregate time effects to individual behaviors.  Second, we jointly model, along with
6:175:12869:22:22:72:540:313:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:the evolution of body mass and wages, several individual behaviors that may be affected by
6:176:12959:22:22:72:540:291:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:body mass and that also explain observed wage variation.  Hence, we are able to decompose
6:177:13048:22:22:72:540:269:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:the correlation between body mass and wages reported in the literature into life-cycle effects
6:178:13142:22:22:72:540:247:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:captured through human capital variables and contemporaneous (direct) influences.  Third,
6:179:13231:22:22:72:540:225:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:we apply dynamic estimation techniques that go beyond traditional methods to better cap-
6:180:13319:22:17:72:540:203:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:ture  impacts  of  interest  over  time  and  across  the  impacted  distribution  and  to  eliminate
6:181:13420:17:16:85:539:186:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:6For example, a permanent individual trait that may affect both body mass and wages (conditional on
6:182:13519:16:12:72:540:170:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:body mass) is self-confidence, which is often unobserved in survey data.  Although used infrequently in this
6:183:13627:12:12:72:540:158:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:literature,  first-differenced  methods  and  panel  data  can  address  endogeneity  that  may  bias  the  measured
6:184:13743:12:12:72:540:146:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:weight/wage relationship.  Receiving even less attention has been the role of time-varying unobservables that
6:185:13852:12:12:72:540:134:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:affect both body mass and employment or wages.  For example, an unobserved temporary negative health
6:186:13952:12:12:72:540:122:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:event (e.g., a broken leg) may lead to weight gain as well as a reduction in observed wages if the researcher
6:187:14061:12:8:72:487:110:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:cannot fully account for accumulation (or depreciation) of human capital (e.g., work absences).
6:188:14156:8:16:85:539:102:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:7There is evidence in the literature that women understate their weight (Lakdawalla and Philipson, 2002;
6:189:14260:16:12:72:540:86:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:Chen, 2012).  Some authors in this literature have re-scaled self-reported height and weight following Cawley
6:190:14369:12:0:72:458:74:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:(2004); others have used different measures of adiposity (Burkhauser and Cawley, 2008).
7:191:14457:0:22:72:540:708:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:bias in both the observed and unobserved dimensions.  More specifically, we make few dis-
7:192:14546:22:22:72:540:686:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:tributional assumptions about the jointly-correlated unobservables; rather, we discretize the
7:193:14639:22:22:72:540:664:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:distributions and estimate the event-specific impacts, mass points, and weights of these un-
7:194:14731:22:22:72:540:642:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:observed  factors  using  a  flexible,  discrete  factor  random  effects  method.   We  also  seek  to
7:195:14834:22:22:72:540:620:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:understand the impact of body mass on the full support of wages, not just the first moment.
7:196:14925:22:22:72:540:598:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:That is, we allow body mass (and other variables) to influence wages differently at different
7:197:15018:22:22:72:540:576:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:points  of  support  of  the  wage  distribution  by  modeling  the  density  of  wages  without  im-
7:198:15119:22:22:72:540:554:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:posing a distributional assumption.  Similarly, we allow determinants of body mass to have
7:199:15209:22:22:72:540:532:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:different effects at different points of the support of the body mass distribution.  We estimate
7:200:15305:22:22:72:540:510:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:the conditional densities for wages and body mass jointly with endogenous behaviors that
7:201:15393:22:22:72:540:488:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:determine both wages and body mass, allowing permanent and time-varying observed and
7:202:15477:22:21:72:318:466:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:unobserved heterogeneity to affect all outcomes.
7:203:15525:21:22:100:540:445:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Using  our  research  sample  consisting  of  twenty  years  of  annual  observations  on  the
7:204:15619:22:22:72:540:423:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:same individuals, we have replicated, with similar specifications and estimation techniques,
7:205:15711:22:22:72:540:401:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:the results in the literature.  The biased findings suggest that current body mass, measured
7:206:15803:22:22:72:540:379:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:by the conventional body mass index (BMI), has a statistically significant negative effect on
7:207:15896:22:22:72:540:357:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:the current wages of white women.  We find a smaller, but statistically significant effect on
7:208:15989:22:22:72:540:335:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:the  wages  of  black  women  in  the  most  sparse  specifications.   When  we  take  advantage  of
7:209:16089:22:22:72:540:313:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:the longitudinal nature of the data, and estimate a model with fixed individual effects, the
7:210:16181:22:22:72:540:291:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:upwardly-biased significant effect of BMI on wages of white women falls, but is still signifi-
7:211:16275:22:22:72:540:269:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:cant.  The effect for black women in a model that allows for permanent individual unobserved
7:212:16367:22:22:72:540:247:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:heterogeneity actually reverses signs.  We move beyond the standard approaches in the liter-
7:213:16459:22:22:72:540:225:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:ature to jointly estimate 17 equations that capture the dynamic life-cycle behavior described
7:214:16552:22:21:72:540:203:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:above and that allow for selection into employment, endogeneity of several determinants of
7:215:16642:21:22:72:540:182:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:wages, and measurement error.  Here, calculation of the marginal effect of BMI on wages is
7:216:16732:22:22:72:540:160:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:not so straightforward because of its contemporaneous and life-cycle effects captured by the
7:217:16824:22:22:72:540:138:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:dynamic model.  We quantify those marginal effects through simulation using the estimated
7:218:16913:22:22:72:540:116:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:model.  We find a different effect of BMI at different levels of wages, which is captured by the
7:219:17009:22:22:72:540:94:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:conditional density estimation procedure.  More specifically, the impact of a contemporane-
7:220:17100:22:0:72:540:72:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:ous improvement in body mass on wages of white women is positive and increases over the
8:221:17188:0:22:72:540:708:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:support of the distribution of wages.  That is, the largest impacts occur at the highest deciles
8:222:17284:22:22:72:540:686:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:of wages.  For black women, the contemporaneous weight-related wage penalty is smaller and
8:223:17374:22:22:72:540:664:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:is actually negative at lower wages,  yet approaches that of white women at higher wages.
8:224:17463:22:22:72:540:642:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:When we account for the impact of better health (i.e., a lower body mass) over the life cycle
8:225:17556:22:22:72:540:620:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:on  other  determinants  of  wages,  we  find  that  the  total  effect  on  wages  is  slightly  smaller
8:226:17661:22:22:72:540:598:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:than that implied by the contemporaneous effect.  The life-cycle impact of body mass (which
8:227:17752:22:22:72:540:576:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:includes the contemporaneous effect) is significantly different from zero for all women over
8:228:17844:22:57:72:182:554:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:the support of wages.
8:229:17865:57:33:72:391:497:17:g_d0_f2:17:g_d0_f2:2  Review of the Relevant Literature
8:230:17901:33:22:72:540:464:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:The economics literature examining wage determinants and wage disparities is large.  Rather
8:231:17992:22:22:72:540:442:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:than discuss this literature, we focus on research that explores the impact of health on wages,
8:232:18087:22:22:72:540:420:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:with  health  specifically  measured  by  body  mass.   We  also  briefly  discuss  the  relationship
8:233:18188:22:22:72:540:398:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:between body mass and other determinants of employment and wages (Cawley and Ruhm,
8:234:18270:22:48:72:103:376:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:2012).
8:235:18276:48:30:72:499:328:14:g_d0_f2:14:g_d0_f2:2.1  Measured Associations between Body Mass and Wages
8:236:18330:30:22:72:540:298:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:An  association  between  obesity  and  labor  market  outcomes,  particularly  wages,  has  been
8:237:18427:22:22:72:540:276:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:documented in the economic literature.  The majority of these studies estimate a reduced-
8:238:18516:22:22:72:540:254:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:form  static  model  of  the  relationship  between  body  mass  and  wages.   A  few  exceptions
8:239:18613:22:21:72:540:232:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:explore the underlying mechanisms behind the relationship (Harper, 2000; Baum and Ford,
8:240:18700:21:22:72:540:211:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:2004; Bhattacharya and Bundorf, 2004; Carpenter, 2006; Han, Norton, and Stearns, 2009;
8:241:18786:22:22:72:540:189:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Han, Norton, and Powell, 2009).  Harper (2000) shows a positive effect of being physically
8:242:18876:22:22:72:540:167:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:attractive on the probability of working in managerial or professional specialty and clerical
8:243:18969:22:22:72:540:145:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:occupations for women.  However, physical attractiveness is not found to be associated with
8:244:19060:22:22:72:540:123:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:sorting into customer-oriented occupations and also no wage differences are found between
8:245:19149:22:22:72:540:101:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:non-attractive  and  attractive  women  in  customer-oriented  jobs  in  the  same  study.   Baum
8:246:19246:22:22:72:540:79:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:and Ford (2004) examine four potential pathways linking obesity to labor market outcomes:
8:247:19335:22:0:72:540:57:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:less productivity due to health problems from obesity (measured by health limitations), less
9:248:19428:0:22:72:540:708:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:investment  in  human  capital  by  obese  workers  (measured  by  work  experience),  employer
9:249:19523:22:22:72:540:686:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:distaste for obese employees due to high health care cost (measured by employer provision
9:250:19612:22:22:72:540:664:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:of health insurance), and consumer distaste for obese workers (measured by classification of
9:251:19704:22:22:72:540:642:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:occupation as customer-related).  Bhattacharya and Bundorf (2004) report no statistically
9:252:19793:22:22:72:540:620:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:significant wage differential between obese and non-obese individuals by employer provided
9:253:19883:22:22:72:540:598:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:health insurance coverage.  Carpenter (2006) shows that the employment rate increased four
9:254:19973:22:22:72:540:576:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:percent for obese women and two percent for obese men compared to their respective non-
9:255:20060:22:22:72:540:554:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:obese counterparts after a 1993 court case (Cook vs. Rhode Island) in which a federal appeals
9:256:20153:22:22:72:540:532:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:court ruled that obesity is covered under the Rehabilitation Act of 1973 and the Americans
9:257:20243:22:22:72:540:510:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:with Disabilities Act.  Han, Norton, and Stearns (2009) report larger negative relationships
9:258:20335:22:22:72:540:488:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:between adult contemporaneous body mass and wages in occupations requiring interpersonal
9:259:20423:22:21:72:540:466:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:skills.  Han, Norton, and Powell (2009) examine the direct effect of body mass on wages and
9:260:20514:21:22:72:540:445:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:the indirect effects operating through education and occupation choice.  They find that body
9:261:20606:22:22:72:540:423:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:weight in the upper tail of the distribution significantly affects educational and occupational
9:262:20701:22:22:72:540:401:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:outcomes of men and women, which in turn have significant effects on wages.  The 11 percent
9:263:20792:22:22:72:540:379:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:adult wage penalty of obesity during a woman’s late teen years can be partly attributed to
9:264:20882:22:22:72:540:357:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:the effect of body mass on human capital accumulation.  While almost all empirical work
9:265:20969:22:22:72:540:335:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:on this subject estimates ordinary least squares models with or without attempts to address
9:266:21060:22:22:72:540:313:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:endogeneity of body mass, Johar and Katayama (2012) use quantile regression to assess the
9:267:21149:22:22:72:540:291:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:role of body mass at different wage levels.  They find that the body mass wage penalty is
9:268:21238:22:22:72:471:269:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:larger at higher wages for white women and at median wages for white males.
9:269:21313:22:22:100:540:247:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Regardless of the estimation techniques in the previous economic studies, most studies
9:270:21399:22:22:72:540:225:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:report gender differences in the association between obesity and wages.  Women consistently
9:271:21490:22:21:72:540:203:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:show  a  statistically  significant  negative  association  of  BMI  or  obesity  with  hourly  wages
9:272:21591:21:22:72:540:182:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:(Averett  and  Korenman  1996;  Cawley  2004;  Baum  and  Ford  2004;  Conley  and  Glauber
9:273:21682:22:22:72:540:160:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:2005; Han, Norton, and Stearns 2009).  BMI is also associated with women’s total household
9:274:21772:22:22:72:540:138:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:income by affecting their spouses’ earnings and occupational prestige (Conley and Glauber,
9:275:21862:22:22:72:540:116:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:2005).  Some studies also report racial differences in the association between body mass and
9:276:21954:22:22:72:540:94:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:labor  market  outcomes  for  women.   For  example,  Cawley  (2004)  reports  that  only  white
9:277:22050:22:0:72:422:72:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:women’s hourly wages are causally associated with their body mass.
10:278:22117:0:30:72:277:708:14:g_d0_f2:14:g_d0_f2:2.2  Endogenous Body Mass
10:279:22142:30:22:72:540:678:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Only a few of the studies attempt to address the potential endogeneity of body mass, typically
10:280:22236:22:22:72:540:656:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:measured by BMI or an obesity indicator (Averett and Korenman, 1996; Pagan and Davila,
10:281:22322:22:22:72:540:634:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:1997;  Behrman  and  Rosenzweig,  2001;  Cawley,  2004;  Baum  and  Ford,  2004;  Conley  and
10:282:22415:22:22:72:540:612:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Glauber, 2005; Norton and Han, 2008; Johar and Katayama, 2012; Sabia and Rees, 2012;
10:283:22499:22:22:72:540:590:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:and Pinkston, 2015).  Instrumental variable estimation techniques require a variable that is
10:284:22591:22:22:72:540:568:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:correlated with body mass in the current period but uncorrelated with wages in the current
10:285:22681:22:22:72:540:546:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:period.  Instrumental variables (IVs) used in this literature include a lag of respondents’ own
10:286:22776:22:21:72:540:524:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:body weight or BMI (Averett and Korenman, 1996; Conley and Glauber, 2005), or siblings’
10:287:22863:21:22:72:540:503:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:or  children’s  BMI  (Cawley,  2000;  Cawley,  2004;  Johar  and  Karayama,  2012).   The  lag  of
10:288:22961:22:22:72:540:481:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:current BMI is not a valid instrument if there is any serial or inter-temporal correlation in
10:289:23054:22:22:72:540:459:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:the wage residuals.  Likewise, children’s body weight is not a valid instrument if unobserved
10:290:23147:22:22:72:540:437:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:heterogeneity in the wage residual is associated with both children’s body weight and the
10:291:23236:22:22:72:540:415:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:mother’s employment behavior.  Also, siblings’ BMI is not a valid instrument if siblings share
10:292:23330:22:22:72:540:393:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:unobserved endowment factors that influence earnings.  Norton and Han (2008) use genetic
10:293:23418:22:22:72:540:371:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:variation among young adults to identify their model (using multiple instruments). They find
10:294:23510:22:22:72:540:349:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:no statistically significant effect of adolescents’ BMI on wages in young adulthood.  There are
10:295:23605:22:22:72:540:327:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:potential limitations of using own genetic information as IVs for own behavioral outcomes
10:296:23694:22:22:72:540:305:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:given that genes that predict body weight outcomes are related to brain chemistry and, thus,
10:297:23786:22:22:72:540:283:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:are  likely  to  affect  labor  market  outcomes  such  as  wages  via  various  channels.   Pinkston
10:298:23887:22:21:72:540:261:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:(2015) estimates the impacts of current and lagged BMI on wages using a differenced GMM
10:299:23974:21:49:72:490:240:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:estimator which relies on additional lags of body mass as identifying instruments.
10:300:24056:49:30:72:341:191:14:g_d0_f2:14:g_d0_f2:2.3  Body Mass and Other Outcomes
10:301:24089:30:22:72:540:161:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:The growing interest among economists in the dynamic effects of body mass on many socio-
10:302:24177:22:22:72:540:139:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:economic  outcomes  (e.g,  employment,  schooling,  marriage)  emphasizes  the  importance  of
10:303:24271:22:22:72:540:117:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:understanding  the  effects  of  body  mass  on  the  accumulated  determinants  of  wages  (i.e.,
10:304:24369:22:22:72:540:95:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:human capital and productivity) over the life cycle.  Some studies assess the association of
10:305:24461:22:0:72:540:73:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:obesity with labor market outcomes at the extensive margin by measuring its effect on the
11:306:24551:0:22:72:540:708:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:probability  of  employment  or  the  different  effect  by  different  job  sectors.   Cawley  (2000)
11:307:24653:22:22:72:540:686:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:finds no statistically significant effect of obesity on the amount of paid work and limitations
11:308:24748:22:22:72:540:664:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:on  types  of  paid  work.   Morris  (2007)  estimates  a  negative  relationship  between  obesity
11:309:24847:22:22:72:540:642:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:and  the  probability  of  employment  for  British  people  for  both  genders.   Paraponaris  and
11:310:24946:22:22:72:540:620:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:colleagues (2005) report that a one standard deviation increase of BMI from the mean at
11:311:25033:22:22:72:540:598:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:age 20 raises the percentage of time spent unemployed during the working years and lowers
11:312:25122:22:22:72:540:576:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:the  probability  of  employment  after  a  period  of  unemployment  for  both  genders.   Pagan
11:313:25219:22:22:72:540:554:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:and Davila’s (1997) study reports that both obese men and women are less likely to sort
11:314:25306:22:22:72:540:532:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:into managerial, professional and technical occupations among fourteen Census occupation
11:315:25394:22:22:72:125:510:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:categories.
11:316:25405:22:22:100:540:488:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:There are also a few papers estimating the effect of obesity on educational achievement.
11:317:25493:22:21:72:540:466:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Some studies report no effect of obesity on grade point average among adolescents (Cros-
11:318:25581:21:22:72:540:445:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:noe  and  Muller,  2004),  scores  on  Peabody  Individual  Achievement  Tests  among  pre-teen
11:319:25676:22:22:72:540:423:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:children (Kaestner and Grossman, 2009), and grade progression and drop out status (Kaest-
11:320:25765:22:22:72:540:401:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:ner,  Grossman,  and  Yarnoff,  2009)  among  adolescents.   In  contrast,  Sabia  (2007)  reports
11:321:25863:22:22:72:540:379:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:that white female adolescents have a grade point average penalty for being obese, whereas
11:322:25952:22:22:72:315:357:12:g_d0_f3:8:g_d0_f4:non-white female and male adolescents do not.8
11:323:25998:22:22:100:548:335:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:The role of body mass on marriage behavior has received much attention from economists.
11:324:26085:22:22:72:540:313:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Using a model of the marriage market, Chiappori, et al.  (2012) estimate the trade-offs (in
11:325:26176:22:22:72:540:291:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:terms of income and education) that men and women are willing to accept to compensate
11:326:26261:22:22:72:540:269:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:for body mass penalties.  Malcolm and Kaya (2014) argue that an observed negative cor-
11:327:26347:22:22:72:540:247:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:relation between BMI and marriage is not the only consequence of a lower desirability for
11:328:26436:22:22:72:540:225:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:individuals with higher BMI, which reduces their competitiveness in the marriage market.
11:329:26524:22:21:72:540:203:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:They  demonstrate  that  individuals  with  higher  BMI  who  marry  do  so  at  a  younger  age.
11:330:26621:21:22:72:540:182:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Averett, et al.  (2013) show that the direction of causality also flows in the reverse direction;
11:331:26718:22:22:72:540:160:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:that is, marriage impacts the health of individuals.  Lin et al.(2014) show that sex ratios are
11:332:26813:22:18:72:211:138:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:correlated with body mass.
11:333:26839:18:16:85:539:120:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:8Sabia’s  work  provides  another  example  of  attempts  to  account  for  endogneous  BMI.  He  uses  parent-
11:334:26950:16:12:72:540:104:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:reported  self-classification  of  own  obesity  as  an  IV  for  adolescent  BMI,  which  is  not  likely  to  be  a  valid
11:335:27074:12:12:72:540:92:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:IV if the parents’ self-classification reflects the level of their self-esteem or time preference which may also
11:336:27186:12:0:72:291:80:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:influence their children’s educational achievement.
12:337:27238:0:30:72:307:708:14:g_d0_f2:14:g_d0_f2:2.4  Measurement of Body Mass
12:338:27267:30:22:72:540:678:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:The  body  mass  index  is  a  simple  means  for  classifying  sedentary  individuals  by  weight:
12:339:27366:22:22:72:540:656:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:underweight,  ideal  weight,  overweight,  and  obese.9The  original  proponents  of  this  index
12:340:27463:22:22:71:539:634:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:stressed  its  advantageous  use  in  making  population  comparisons.   They  warned  against
12:341:27557:22:22:71:539:612:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:using it for individual health diagnosis.  BMI, as a function of weight and height, does not
12:342:27649:22:22:71:539:590:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:allow  one  to  distinguish  between  fat  mass  and  fat-free  mass  (such  as  muscle  and  bone).
12:343:27749:22:22:71:539:568:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:It  may  overestimate  adiposity  in  those  with  more  lean  body  mass  (e.g.,  athletes),  while
12:344:27849:22:22:71:478:546:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:underestimating adiposity on those with less lean body mass (e.g., the elderly).
12:345:27929:22:21:100:540:524:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Different methods of assessing overweight and obesity may allow researchers to capture
12:346:28015:21:22:71:539:503:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:adiposity (or body weight) as it relates to productivity versus appearance.  For example, fat
12:347:28108:22:22:71:539:481:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:mass can be measured by percentage of body fat (using skinfold, underwater weighing, or
12:348:28195:22:22:71:539:459:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:fat-free mass techniques) or by measures that account for mass and volume location (waist
12:349:28284:22:22:71:539:437:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:and arm circumference and the body volume index).  Johansson,  et al.  (2009) and Wada
12:350:28370:22:22:71:539:415:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:and  Tekin (2010) show  that the  measure  of adiposity  affects analysis  of  the obesity wage
12:351:28465:22:22:71:539:393:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:penalty.  Burkhauser and Cawley (2008) find that obesity measured by BMI is only weakly
12:352:28552:22:22:71:539:371:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:correlated with obesity defined by other measures of fatness.  Unfortunately, however, many
12:353:28643:22:22:71:539:349:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:data sets that contain information on employment and wages contain only measures of height
12:354:28733:22:22:71:539:327:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:and weight that can be used to construct the body mass index.  Another concern is that
12:355:28819:22:22:71:539:305:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:the data on height and weight are often self-reported.  These self-reports are likely to suffer
12:356:28914:22:22:71:539:283:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:from reporting error, subjectivity error, and rounding error.  Because it is necessary for us
12:357:29007:22:21:71:539:261:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:to rely on the traditional BMI index to measure adiposity, we use an estimation technique
12:358:29096:21:58:71:301:240:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:that addresses potential measurement errors.
12:359:29140:58:34:71:403:182:17:g_d0_f2:17:g_d0_f2:3  Description of the Research Sample
12:360:29177:34:22:71:539:148:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:The  National  Longitudinal  Survey  of  Youth  (NLSY)  provides  data  on  a  1979  cohort  of
12:361:29272:22:22:71:539:126:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:12,686 individuals aged 14 to 22 followed annually through 1994.  These individuals continue
12:362:29364:22:18:71:539:104:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:to be followed every two years after 1996 (with data available currently through 2012).  Our
12:363:29456:18:16:85:539:86:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:9Specifially, BMI equals weight in kilograms divided by height in meters squared.  A BMI less than or
12:364:29557:16:12:72:540:70:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:equal to 18.5 indicates underweight; between 18.5 and 25, normal weight; greater than or equal to 25 and
12:365:29661:12:0:72:290:58:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:less than 30, overweight; and 30 or greater, obese.
13:366:29714:0:22:72:540:708:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:research sample includes those who have not attrited by 1983 and who have responses for
13:367:29801:22:22:72:540:686:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:important variables in our analysis.  Because the literature on the relationship between body
13:368:29894:22:22:72:540:664:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:mass  and  wages  has  detected  a  wage  effect  that  differs  between  black  and  white  females,
13:369:29995:22:22:72:540:642:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:we  restrict  our  analysis  in  this  paper  to  women  of  these  races.   We  drop  sample-eligible
13:370:30097:22:22:72:540:620:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:individuals  who  report  an  Asian  race  or  Hispanic  ethnicity.   From  1983,  we  follow  3,213
13:371:30197:22:22:72:540:598:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:females  through  2002  or  until  they  attrit  from  the  NLSY  survey.   Table  1  displays  the
13:372:30296:22:22:72:539:576:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:research  sample  size  by  year  and  the  percent  of  individuals  who  attrit  each  year.10The
13:373:30395:22:28:72:368:554:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:research sample contains 51,884 person-year observations.

13:392:30745:13:13:197:396:250:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:19972,264703.09
13:393:30760:13:13:197:396:237:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:19982,194582.64
13:394:30775:13:13:197:396:224:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:19992,1361145.34
13:395:30791:13:13:197:396:211:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:20002,022422.08
13:396:30806:13:13:197:396:198:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:20011,9801025.15
13:397:30822:13:26:197:387:185:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:20021,878--
13:398:30833:26:46:197:364:159:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Person-year observations:  51,884
13:399:30866:46:15:81:539:113:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:10We restrict the initial 1983 sample to include individuals who are observed for at least two consecutive
13:400:30972:15:12:72:540:98:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:periods.   Hence,  attrition  does  not  occur  between  1983  and  1984.   In  fact,  the  1983  data  serve  as  initial
13:401:31094:12:12:72:540:86:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:conditions for the subsequent period of observation.  Additionally, we have no need to model attrition at the
13:402:31203:12:0:72:324:74:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:end of 2002 since this is the last year of data that we use.
14:403:31265:0:22:100:540:708:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:The NLSY data provide a unique understanding of individual body mass dynamics over
14:404:31347:22:22:72:540:686:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:time because the survey follows the same individuals for such a long time (e.g., over 20 years
14:405:31441:22:22:72:539:664:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:in some cases) relative to most data sets.11Since the evolution of body mass as individuals
14:406:31532:22:22:72:540:642:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:age  is  important  in  this  study,  we  depict  the  trends  in  body  mass  over  time  in  Figure  1.
14:407:31637:22:22:72:540:620:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Average body mass increases by age for women of both races, with the mean increasing from
14:408:31726:22:22:72:540:598:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:21.5 and 22.7 at age 20 to 25.9 and 29.8 at age 40, for white and black females respectively.
14:409:31819:22:22:72:540:576:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:More importantly, the value of the 75th percentile of the BMI distribution is increasing and
14:410:31911:22:22:72:540:554:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:the right tail of the distribution is larger as individuals age.  .12Across all ages, one-year
14:411:32005:22:22:72:540:532:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:body mass deviations (not pictured) average 0.21 and 0.32 BMI units for white and black
14:412:32092:22:22:72:540:510:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:females respectively.  For an average height female (i.e., 5’ 4”), this amounts to an increase
14:413:32186:22:22:72:540:488:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:of 1.2 to 1.9 pounds per year.  Despite the fact that much of the interquartile range of BMI
14:414:32278:22:21:72:540:466:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:deviations from one year to another lies above zero at each age, the median BMI deviation
14:415:32367:21:22:72:108:445:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:is zero.
14:416:32375:22:22:100:540:423:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Since we intend to explore how wages vary with body mass, we emphasize that body
14:417:32455:22:22:72:540:401:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:mass increases with age, on average, in the 18 to 45 year age range.  It is also the case that
14:418:32549:22:22:72:540:379:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:the strongest predictors of wages — education and work experience — also increase with age.
14:419:32640:22:22:72:540:357:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:In general, while additional education may be obtained at any age, it is often completed in
14:420:32731:22:22:72:540:335:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:the early twenties.  Work experience, however, accumulates over the life cycle.  If individuals
14:421:32826:22:22:72:540:313:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:gain weight as they age,  but are also gaining work experience as they age,  a depiction of
14:422:32917:22:22:72:540:291:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:wages by age will almost surely reveal that those with larger body mass also have higher
14:423:33005:22:22:72:540:269:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:wages.   Hence,  it  is  important  to  control  for  human  capital  variables  (i.e.,  education  and
14:424:33108:22:22:72:540:247:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:work experience) that increase with age, as well as other cumulative variables that influence
14:425:33201:22:22:72:540:225:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:productivity (i.e., marital status and tenure, number of children in the household, and body
14:426:33293:22:18:72:540:203:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:mass).  Because these important wage determinants may be influenced by body mass and,
14:427:33378:18:16:81:539:185:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:11Weight and height are observed in the NLSY79 data in most years with the exception of 1983, 1984,
14:428:33477:16:12:72:540:169:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:1991, and the non-survey years of 1995, 1997, 1999, and 2001.  We linearly interpolate or extrapolate values
14:429:33585:12:12:72:540:157:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:of weight in years that we do not observe weight.  We assume that height is the same across all years an
14:430:33689:12:12:72:540:145:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:individual is observed.  Using these height and weight values,  we construct the body mass index for each
14:431:33794:12:8:72:265:133:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:year an individual is in our research sample.
14:432:33839:8:15:81:539:125:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:12The horizontal lines in Figure 1 indicate upper thresholds for underweight (18.5), normal (25), and over-
14:433:33946:15:12:72:540:110:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:weight (30) with a BMI greater than 30 indicating obese.  The dark shaded regions indicate the interquartile
14:434:34054:12:12:72:540:98:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:range.  The light shaded regions extend up to 1.5 times the interquartile range.  Points on either side of the
14:435:34164:12:0:72:255:86:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:shaded areas represent remaining outliers.
15:436:34208:0:0:146:464:218:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Figure 1:  Distribution of Body Mass as Females Age, by Race
16:437:34270:0:22:72:540:708:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:inversely, influence body mass, we model their dynamic evolutions jointly while allowing for
16:438:34362:22:22:72:416:686:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:common observed and unobserved heterogeneity over the life cycle.
16:439:34427:22:22:100:540:664:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Educational  attainment  and  work  experience  are  determined  by  schooling  and  work
16:440:34516:22:22:72:540:642:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:behaviors  at  each  age.   Figure  2  displays  these  probabilities  by  age  and  by  predominant
16:441:34616:22:22:72:539:620:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:body mass status.13We model the probability of part-time employment or non-employment
16:442:34701:22:22:72:540:598:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:relative to full-time employment, and depict full-time employment probabilities by race and
16:443:34792:22:405:72:212:576:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:weight status in this figure.
16:444:34821:405:36:105:506:171:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Figure 2:  Schooling and Employment Statistics by Age, Race, and Body Mass
16:445:34895:36:22:100:540:135:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Marital  status  and  fertility  may  change  as  one  ages.   These  annual  behaviors  define
16:446:34990:22:18:72:540:113:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:accumulated stocks (namely, marital tenure and number of children in the household) that
16:447:35078:18:16:81:539:95:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:13These graphs are based on those individuals in the research sample who are observed every year from
16:448:35179:16:12:72:540:79:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:age 25 to age 40.  The normal and overweight distinction reflects the body mass status of an individual in
16:449:35285:12:0:72:216:67:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:more than half of those 16 years.
17:450:35320:0:22:72:540:708:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:may shift preferences for schooling and employment.  They may also impact productivity at
17:451:35409:22:22:72:540:686:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:work conditional on employment, which may be reflected in wages.  Rather than focus only
17:452:35497:22:22:72:540:664:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:on births as a mechanism by which household size may change, we broaden the process to in-
17:453:35587:22:22:72:540:642:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:clude increases through birth, adoption, and blended families and reductions through death,
17:454:35678:22:22:72:540:620:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:marriage dissolution, removal, and matriculation.  In addition to the effects children in the
17:455:35771:22:22:72:540:598:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:household may have on employment behavior and productivity, children may impact body
17:456:35855:22:22:72:540:576:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:mass of the parents.  Most obviously, childbearing imposes physical changes in a woman’s
17:457:35943:22:22:72:540:554:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:body mass that may not be temporary.  Additionally, children impose both time and finan-
17:458:36031:22:22:72:540:532:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:cial requirements that may alter caloric intake and expenditure of their caregivers.  Figure 3
17:459:36125:22:22:72:540:510:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:describes marriage and child accumulation probabilities for females by predominant body
17:460:36212:22:402:72:135:488:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:mass status.
17:461:36224:402:0:117:495:86:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Figure 3:  Marriage and Children Statistics by Age, Race, and Body Mass
18:462:36297:0:34:72:283:708:17:g_d0_f2:17:g_d0_f2:4  Empirical Framework
18:463:36319:34:22:72:540:674:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Our  empirical  framework  is  motivated  by  a  theoretical  model  of  life-cycle  decisionmaking
18:464:36418:22:22:72:540:652:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:and  health  production:  per-period  decisions  regarding  education,  employment,  marriage,
18:465:36512:22:22:72:540:630:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:and children with uncertain future wages and body mass transitions.  The theory allows us
18:466:36601:22:22:72:540:608:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:to  derive choice  probabilities  that we approximate  using dynamic,  non-linear  functions  of
18:467:36697:22:22:72:540:586:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:the information available (to an individual) at the beginning of each decisionmaking period.
18:468:36789:22:22:72:540:564:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:The  theory  also  describes  how  these  period-by-period  decisions  affect  the  production  of
18:469:36887:22:22:72:540:542:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:health  (or  evolution  of  body  mass)  over  time  and  the  implied  distribution  of  wages.   We
18:470:36988:22:22:72:540:520:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:then  estimate  the  approximated  demand  and  production  equations  jointly,  and  allow  for
18:471:37084:22:21:72:540:498:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:both  permanent  and  time-varying  individual  unobserved  heterogeneity  (UH)  through  the
18:472:37177:21:22:72:540:477:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:use of discrete factor random effects (Heckman and Singer, 1983 and Mroz, 1999).  Rather
18:473:37265:22:22:72:540:455:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:than estimate the conditional mean of wages and body mass, we estimate the conditional
18:474:37351:22:22:72:540:433:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:density of these observed outcomes using a conditional density estimation (CDE) technique
18:475:37440:22:22:72:211:411:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:(Gilleskie and Mroz, 2004).
18:476:37467:22:22:100:540:389:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:This multi-faceted empirical approach enables us to consider aspects of the problem
18:477:37550:22:22:72:540:367:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:that may not be computationally-feasible with full-solution and estimation of the dynamic
18:478:37639:22:22:72:540:345:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:optimization problem.  We 1.)  include several endogenous explanatory variables that provide
18:479:37731:22:22:72:540:323:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:a detailed description of the histories of individual decisions over the life cycle, 2.)  consider
18:480:37829:22:22:72:540:301:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:the continuous evolution of body mass rather than discrete categories, and 3.)  explore the
18:481:37920:22:22:72:540:279:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:possibility that variables of interest may have different marginal effects at different points of
18:482:38017:22:22:72:540:257:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:support of the wage and body mass distributions.  We seek to evaluate the causal pathways
18:483:38106:22:21:72:540:235:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:of body mass on wages of women (either directly or indirectly through other life-cycle de-
18:484:38196:21:22:72:540:214:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:terminants) and to distinguish the contemporaneous effect from that generated by life-cycle
18:485:38287:22:0:72:254:192:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:behaviors influenced by body mass.
19:486:38323:0:30:72:259:708:14:g_d0_f2:14:g_d0_f2:4.1  Timing and Notation
19:487:38347:30:22:72:540:678:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:In each yeartafter age 18, an individual obtains a wage offer drawn from the population
19:488:38434:22:22:72:539:656:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:distribution of wages.  The individual also observes her spouse’s wage if married.14The wage
19:489:38526:22:22:72:539:634:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:a female would receive if she worked,wt, depends on education and employment experience,
19:490:38614:22:22:71:539:612:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:as described by Mincer (1974).  We argue that these human capital variables may not fully
19:491:38703:22:22:71:539:590:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:capture a person’s productivity at work.  Her productivity, or effort, might be influenced by
19:492:38796:22:22:71:539:568:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:her health or her time demands.  Thus, we include body mass (Cawley, 2004), marital status
19:493:38886:22:22:71:539:546:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f8:(Korenman and Neumark, 1991), and number of children in the household entering periodt
19:494:38972:22:21:72:290:524:12:g_d0_f3:8:g_d0_f4:as determinants of the wage distribution.15
19:495:39015:21:22:100:540:503:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Each period the individual then jointly decides 1.)  whether or not to attend school,
19:496:39100:22:22:71:539:481:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:2.)  whether to be non-employed, part-time employed, or full-time employed, 3.)  whether or
19:497:39191:22:22:71:539:459:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:not to be married, and 4.)  whether or not to acquire (or lose) a child (or children) living
19:498:39283:22:22:71:539:437:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:in the household.  We let indicator variables (lower case) define the alternatives.  That is,
19:499:39376:22:22:71:539:415:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f3:st= 1 if the individual attends school in periodtandst= 0, otherwise.  The employment
19:500:39461:22:22:72:540:393:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:indicator takes on the valueet= 0 if the individual does not work in periodt,et= 1 if he
19:501:39549:22:22:72:540:371:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:works part time (less than 1375 hours per year), andet= 2 if he works full time (1375 hours
19:502:39640:22:22:72:539:349:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:or more per year).  We indicate the marriage decision bymt= 1 if the individual is married
19:503:39730:22:22:72:540:327:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:in yeart, andmt= 0, otherwise.  The number of children in the household may increase or
19:504:39817:22:22:72:540:305:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:decrease due to pregnancy (singleton or multiples), marriage, divorce, age of child, or child
19:505:39910:22:17:72:539:283:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:mortality.16The variablekt= 0 indicates that there is no change in the number of children
19:506:39999:17:16:81:539:266:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:14Spouse income is treated as exogenous.  While we model the marriage decision, which may be influenced
19:507:40102:16:12:72:540:250:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:by body mass, we do not model the correlation between an individual’s body mass and her potential spouse’s
19:508:40208:12:8:72:214:238:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:income (Chiappori, et al., 2012).
19:509:40241:8:16:81:539:230:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:15We recognize that body mass is an imperfect measure of health, and may not fully capture the role that
19:510:40345:16:12:72:540:214:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:health plays in one’s productivity.  Instead, or additionally, it may signal expected medical care expenditures
19:511:40456:12:12:72:540:202:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:(which may be compensated by the employer and hence affect offered wages) or reflect an employer’s tastes
19:512:40561:12:8:72:379:190:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:based on appearance.  We cannot distinguish these roles in estimation.
19:513:40631:8:16:81:539:182:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:16Some reasons for a change in number of children in the household are available in the data.  For example,
19:514:40738:16:12:72:540:166:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:the  survey  records  whether  a  child  was  born,  died,  was  adopted,  etc.   Additionally,  a  comparison  of  the
19:515:40857:12:12:72:540:154:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:number of children in the household from year to year reveals additions and losses of more than one child in
19:516:40965:12:12:72:540:142:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:a non-trivial number of cases.  The changes could be due to birth by the sample female as well as children
19:517:41071:12:12:72:540:130:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:acquired (or lost) through other channels (e.g., adoption, marriage, divorce, aging out, and death).  Because
19:518:41180:12:11:72:540:118:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:of the large number of ways household size can change,  we are motivated to focus only on the change in
19:519:41283:11:12:72:540:107:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:number of children in the household rather than the channel.  A larger household size, regardless of channel,
19:520:41392:12:0:72:477:95:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:places demands on time, finances, and energy, which may impact per-period decisionmaking.
20:521:41483:0:22:72:539:708:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:in the household in yeart, and values ofkt= 1 andkt=−1 indicate that at least one child
20:522:41570:22:22:72:213:686:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:is acquired or lost in yeart.
20:523:41599:22:22:100:540:664:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:These yearly decisions (i.e.,  school attendance,  employment,  marriage,  and children)
20:524:41687:22:22:72:539:642:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:produce stock variables upon entering periodtthat summarize the history of the decisions.
20:525:41776:22:22:72:540:620:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:The vectors of these history variables (upper case) are denoted:  educational history (St),
20:526:41867:22:22:72:540:598:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:work experience (Et), marital history (Mt), and household child accumulation (Kt).  These
20:527:41956:22:22:72:540:576:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:vectors  include  both  duration  values  as  well  as  indicators  of  behavior  or  outcomes  in  the
20:528:42059:22:22:72:539:554:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f8:last period (t−1) and polynomials of the continuous values.  These histories up to periodt
20:529:42149:22:22:72:539:532:12:g_d0_f3:8:g_d0_f4:influence the value of each periodtschooling, employment, marriage, and child alternative.17
20:530:42241:22:22:72:540:510:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:We allow the vector of variables that capture body mass history up to the beginning of a
20:531:42329:22:22:72:539:488:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:period (Bt) to affect current schooling, employment, marital status and child accumulation
20:532:42419:22:21:71:119:466:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:decisions.
20:533:42429:21:22:100:540:445:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Other information known at the beginning of yeartthat may influence preferences,
20:534:42509:22:22:72:540:423:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:constraints, or expectations includes exogenous individual characteristics (e.g., age, gender,
20:535:42603:22:22:72:540:401:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:race,  urbanicity,  region) and exogenous aggregate business cycle indicators (e.g.,  calendar
20:536:42697:22:22:72:539:379:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:year trend variables) denoted by the vectorXt.  Variables that capture known price and sup-
20:537:42788:22:22:72:540:357:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:ply conditions such as average tuition, wage and employment information, welfare amounts,
20:538:42877:22:22:72:540:335:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:restaurant  sales,  and  costs  of  different  food  items  are  denoted  by  the  vectorPt.   We  use
20:539:42979:22:22:72:539:313:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:unique  superscripts  to  denote,  for  descriptive  purposes  only,  the  variables  inPtthat  are
20:540:43078:22:22:72:539:291:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:related to particular endogenous behaviors and outcomes; that is,Pt= (PSt,PEt,PMt,PKt,
20:541:43164:22:22:72:540:269:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f3:PBt).  We recognize, however, that the optimal demand functions for each behavior may de-
20:542:43253:22:22:72:540:247:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:pend on own and cross prices.  The vector of individual information known at the beginning
20:543:43343:22:22:72:345:225:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:of periodtis denoted Ωt= (Bt,St,Et,Mt,Kt,Xt,Pt).
20:544:43391:22:21:100:540:203:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:As forward-looking decisionmakers, the individuals face uncertainty about future wages.
20:545:43478:21:22:72:540:182:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Wages  depend  on  educational  attainment,  work  experience,  and  marital  and  child  histo-
20:546:43574:22:22:72:540:160:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:ries.  In addition to these human capital variables, wages may also be influenced by body
20:547:43663:22:22:72:540:138:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:mass,  which explains variation in wages caused by health impacts on productivity or em-
20:548:43751:22:18:72:540:116:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:ployer preferences about appearance.  Future body mass is also uncertain.  Having made the
20:549:43841:18:16:81:539:98:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:17We are not explicit about how these variables may impact preferences or the budget constraint because
20:550:43944:16:12:72:540:82:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:we do not estimate the primitive parameters of the decisionmaking optimization problem.  However, these
20:551:44047:12:12:72:540:70:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:variables, because they are a part of the information set when evaluating alternatives, are arguments of the
20:552:44155:12:0:72:322:58:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:non-linear demand functions resulting from optimization.
21:553:44213:0:22:72:540:708:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:“beginning-of-period” decisions (i.e., education, employment, marriage, and child accumula-
21:554:44304:22:22:72:540:686:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:tion) that affect the per-period budget and time constraints, we assume that individuals then
21:555:44397:22:22:72:540:664:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:allocate income and time to the daily activities of caloric intake and caloric expenditure (i.e.,
21:556:44494:22:22:72:539:642:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:eating and exercising) denotedci∗tandce∗t.  Theoretically, the density that describes possible
21:557:44588:22:22:72:539:620:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:values of next period’s body mass,Bt+1, depends on body mass this period and these daily
21:558:44676:22:22:72:540:598:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:caloric input and output behaviors during the period.  Unfortunately, these latter activities
21:559:44769:22:22:72:540:576:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:are not observed in our data.  The daily input decisions depend on market and time prices of
21:560:44861:22:22:72:540:554:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:food and exercise,PBt, as well as the less frequently changed behaviors regarding education,
21:561:44953:22:22:72:540:532:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:employment, marital, and child accumulation.  Substituting in the determinants of caloric
21:562:45042:22:22:72:540:510:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:intake and expenditure, expectations of body mass transition are, indirectly, a function of
21:563:45133:22:22:72:540:488:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:the periodtbehaviors, the resulting income (own wages and spouse’s wages if married), and
21:564:45222:22:21:72:178:466:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:relevant prices (PBt).
21:565:45244:21:22:100:540:445:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:The  timeline  below  depicts  the  observed  and  unobserved  outcomes  per  period  (e.g.,
21:566:45336:22:22:72:540:423:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:one year), and the exogenous and endogenous information available at the beginning of each
21:567:45426:22:36:72:108:401:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:period.
21:568:45433:36:14:91:141:365:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:beginning
21:569:45442:14:100:107:125:351:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f8:oft
21:570:45445:100:15:94:139:251:11:g_d0_f6:11:g_d0_f12:Ωt= (Bt,

21:596:45755:38:22:100:540:167:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Variables that explain the observed outcomes are described in Tables 2, 3 and 4 for our
21:597:45842:22:22:72:540:145:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:research sample.  The endogenous variables are summarized over all person-observations in
21:598:45931:22:22:72:540:123:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:the sample over the 1984-2002 period (Table 2).  We begin by describing the variables that
21:599:46021:22:22:72:539:101:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:capture the history of one’s body mass,Bt.  In estimation, we use moments of the continuous
21:600:46112:22:22:71:539:79:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:index  of  body  mass  (BMI)  rather  than  categories  of  adiposity  (i.e.,  underweight,  normal
21:601:46211:22:0:71:539:57:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:weight,  overweight,  and  obese).   We  report  summary  statistics  for  both  for  informational
22:602:46312:0:22:72:540:708:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:purposes.  We also use time-varying indicators of ever overweight or ever obese to capture
22:603:46402:22:22:72:540:686:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:the contemporaneous effects of past weight issues on current behaviors and wages conditional
22:604:46494:22:22:72:540:664:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:on current BMI. Finally,  we include a variable representing how different a person’s BMI
22:605:46583:22:22:72:540:642:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:is from women of the same race in each year.  This measure may reflect social capital,  as
22:606:46673:22:22:72:540:620:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:deviations from the norm in body mass among a specific group capture social perceptions
22:607:46760:22:22:72:539:598:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:of effort or commitment.18An individual’s education history,St, is captured by enrollment
22:608:46849:22:22:71:539:576:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:last period to capture state dependence and the number of years of schooling attainment.
22:609:46937:22:22:71:539:554:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:We  avoid  having  to  include  additional  equations  for  degree  attainment  (available  in  the
22:610:47036:22:22:71:539:532:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:NLSY  data)  by  constructing  indicators  of  at  least  12  years  of  schooling  and  at  least  16
22:611:47138:22:22:71:539:510:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:years of schooling to approximate high school and college degrees, respectively.  An indicator
22:612:47232:22:22:71:539:488:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:for the 13th year of schooling captures potential “freshman fifteen” effects on weight gain.
22:613:47324:22:21:71:540:466:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:The employment history vector,Et, includes indicators of previous employment states and
22:614:47411:21:22:71:539:445:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:years  of  experience,  separated  by  full  and  part  time  status.   The  marital  history  vector,
22:615:47513:22:22:71:539:423:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f3:Mt, includes the previous marriage state, years married if married in the previous period,
22:616:47603:22:22:71:539:401:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:and  years  single  if  ever  married  and  single  in  the  previous  period.   Finally,  the  variables
22:617:47708:22:22:71:540:379:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:summarizing the history of children in the household,Kt, includes the number of children
22:618:47796:22:22:71:539:357:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:in  the  household  entering  the  period,  an  indicator  of  whether  that  number  increased  or
22:619:47895:22:22:71:539:335:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:decreased in the previous period (e.g.,  whether the female gained or lost a child),  and an
22:620:47987:22:22:71:539:313:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:indicator of whether a child addition was within the last 5 years.  Because nearly 90 percent
22:621:48080:22:22:71:539:291:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:of child additions are births, we interpret this variable as an indicator of having a child in
22:622:48174:22:22:71:304:269:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:the household who is less than 6 years of age.
22:623:48220:22:22:100:540:247:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:The exogenous time-invariant individual variables are summarized for the 3213 females
22:624:48305:22:22:71:539:225:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:in our research sample in 1984, while the exogenous time-varying variables are summarized
22:625:48394:22:21:71:539:203:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:over all person-years (Table 3). Own citizenship and parental information are used to identify
22:626:48488:21:22:71:539:182:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:initially-observed values of endogenous variables (discussed in Section 4.6).  Moments and
22:627:48578:22:22:71:539:160:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:interactions of variables are included in estimation.  While we do not allow for a structural
22:628:48671:22:18:71:539:138:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:shift in variables per- and post- the 1993 Cook vs.  Rhode Island federal policy change, we
22:629:48762:18:16:81:539:120:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:18Teh  NLSY79  does  not  include,  ever  wave,  other  measures  of  health  that  would  allow  us  to  measure
22:630:48875:16:12:72:540:104:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:variations in wages by body mass conditional on other measured health indicators (i.e., perhaps a beauty
22:631:48979:12:0:72:183:92:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:effect vs.  a health effect).

24:707:52329:12:12:71:539:77:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:and  year.   We  use  state  and  year  level  variation  in  the  average  Aid  to  Families  with  Dependent  Chil-

25:709:52558:0:22:72:540:708:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:our model (discussed in Section 4.6), these exogenous time-varying variables serve as classic
25:710:52651:22:22:72:540:686:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:identification variables since they are included in theoretically-relevant demand (behavior)
25:711:52743:22:22:72:540:664:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:equations  and  excluded  from  the  body  mass  production  function  conditional  on  included
25:712:52839:22:501:72:436:642:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:endogenous behaviors (as described in more detail in the next section).
25:713:52910:501:12:72:540:141:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:(www.census.gov/popest/data/historical)  with  total  population  at  the  county  level  and  sex  ratios  at
25:714:53020:12:12:72:540:129:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:the  state  level.   Food,  alcohol,  and  cigarette  prices,  from  the  Council  for  Community  and  Economic  Re-
25:715:53137:12:12:72:540:117:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:search (formerly the American Chamber of Commerce Research Association or ACCRA), are aggregated to
25:716:53236:12:12:72:539:105:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:the county level by year (www.c2er.org).  The food price consists of weighted prices of frying chicken (per
25:717:53343:12:12:72:540:93:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:pound), chunk light tuna (5 ou.), half gallon of whole milk, a dozen eggs, margarine (1 lb.), grated parmesan
25:718:53452:12:12:72:540:81:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:cheese (8 ou.), potatoes (10 lb.), bananas (per pound), iceberg lettuce, and white bread.  The junk food price
25:719:53562:12:12:72:540:69:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:is the average price of a McDonald’s quarter pound cheeseburger, a thin crusted cheese pizza at Pizza Hut
