27:754:55324:0:30:72:501:708:14:g_d0_f2:14:g_d0_f2:4.2  Behaviors: Schooling, Employment, Marriage, Children
27:755:55381:30:22:72:540:678:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Below  we  describe  the  dynamic  demand  functions  derived  from  the  joint  schooling,  em-
27:756:55477:22:22:72:540:656:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:ployment, marriage, and children decisions and represented by probabilities of the observed
27:757:55568:22:22:72:540:634:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:outcomes.  While theory suggests that these decisions are jointly made (among 36 mutually
27:758:55657:22:22:72:540:612:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:exclusive combinations of the four behaviors), we empirically model the derived demand as
27:759:55746:22:22:72:540:590:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:four equations and allow the equations to be correlated through time-invariant and time-
27:760:55834:22:22:72:540:568:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:varying observed and unobserved heterogeneity. The error terms that capture the unobserved
27:761:55924:22:22:72:539:546:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:determinants of each equatione,uet,  are decomposed into a permanent individual compo-
27:762:56010:22:21:71:540:524:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:nent  (μ),  a  time-varying  individual  component  (νt),  and  an  idiosyncratic  component  (-t);
27:763:56109:21:22:72:540:503:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:specifically,uet=ρeμ+ωeνt+-et.  Factor loadings (ρandω) in each equation (and for each
27:764:56195:22:22:72:540:481:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:outcome in each equation) indicate the relative importance of the associated type of hetero-
27:765:56287:22:22:72:539:459:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:geneity for that equation or outcome.  The serially-uncorrelated idiosyncratic terms (-et) are
27:766:56381:22:22:71:539:437:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Extreme Value distributed each period, producing logit and multinomial logit probabilities
27:767:56471:22:22:71:228:415:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:of observed decision outcomes.
27:768:56501:22:22:100:539:393:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:The log odds ratio of being enrolled in school in periodt(st= 1) relative to not being
27:769:56587:22:30:71:172:371:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:in school (st= 0) is
27:770:56607:30:0:92:102:341:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:ln
27:771:56609:0:9:104:110:358:12:g_d0_f19:12:g_d0_f19:[
27:772:56610:9:17:111:157:349:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f3:p(st= 1)
27:773:56618:17:0:111:157:332:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f3:p(st= 0)
27:774:56626:0:17:158:164:358:12:g_d0_f19:12:g_d0_f19:]
27:775:56627:17:31:168:540:341:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:=α′fS(Bt,St,Et,Mt,Kt,Xt,PSt,PEt,PMt,PKt,PBt) +ρSμ+ωSνt.(1)
27:776:56685:31:22:72:539:310:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:The log odds ratio of being non-employed (et= 0) or employed part-time (et= 1) relative
27:777:56772:22:30:72:326:288:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:to being employed full-time (et= 2) in periodtis
27:778:56820:30:0:75:85:258:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:ln
27:779:56822:0:9:86:92:275:12:g_d0_f19:12:g_d0_f19:[
27:780:56823:9:16:94:140:266:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f3:p(et=d)
27:781:56830:16:0:94:140:250:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f3:p(et= 2)
27:782:56838:0:17:141:147:275:12:g_d0_f19:12:g_d0_f19:]
27:783:56839:17:22:150:536:258:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f8:=β′dfE(Bt,St,Et,Mt,Kt,Xt,PSt,PEt,PMt,PKt,PBt) +ρEdμ+ωEdνtd= 0,1.
27:784:56903:22:22:525:540:236:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:(2)
27:785:56906:22:22:72:539:214:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:The  log  odds  ratio  of  being  married  in  periodt(mt=  1)  relative  to  not  being  married
27:786:57003:22:30:72:128:192:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:(mt= 0) is
27:787:57013:30:0:85:95:162:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:ln
27:788:57015:0:9:97:103:179:12:g_d0_f19:12:g_d0_f19:[
27:789:57016:9:16:104:155:170:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f3:p(mt= 1)
27:790:57024:16:0:104:155:154:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f3:p(mt= 0)
27:791:57032:0:17:156:162:179:12:g_d0_f19:12:g_d0_f19:]
27:792:57033:17:31:165:540:162:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:=γ′fM(Bt,St,Et,Mt,Kt,Xt,PSt,PEt,PMt,PKt,PBt) +ρMμ+ωMνt.(3)
27:793:57091:31:22:72:539:131:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f8:The  log  odds  ratio  of  a  change  in  the  number  of  children  in  the  household  by  at  leastj
27:794:57194:22:30:72:382:109:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:children in periodt(kt=j) relative to no change (kt= 0) is
27:795:57252:30:0:72:82:79:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:ln
27:796:57254:0:9:83:89:96:12:g_d0_f19:12:g_d0_f19:[
27:797:57255:9:16:91:137:87:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f3:p(kt=j)
27:798:57262:16:0:91:137:71:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f3:p(kt= 0)
27:799:57270:0:17:138:144:96:12:g_d0_f19:12:g_d0_f19:]
27:800:57271:17:22:148:539:79:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f8:=δ′jfK(Bt,St,Et,Mt,Kt,Xt,PSt,PEt,PMt,PKt,PBt) +ρKjμ+ωKjνtj=−1,1.
27:801:57335:22:0:525:540:57:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:(4)
28:802:57340:0:22:100:540:708:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Note that these four probabilities are functions of the same vector of explanatory vari-
28:803:57428:22:22:72:540:686:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:ables since the decisions that produce the observed outcomes are jointly made.  Additionally,
28:804:57521:22:22:72:539:664:12:g_d0_f3:8:g_d0_f9:because the schooling, employment, marriage, and child accumulation decisions (st,et,mt
28:805:57608:22:22:72:539:642:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:andkt) are made jointly at the beginning of the period, all the variables representing supply
28:806:57701:22:22:72:540:620:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:side conditions (PSt,PEt,PMt,PKt) appear in each of Equations 1-4 in order to capture own-
28:807:57791:22:22:71:539:598:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:and  cross-price  effects.   Similarly,  individuals  are  forward  looking  and  anticipate  making
28:808:57891:22:22:71:539:576:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:optimal daily caloric intake and caloric expenditure decisions within the period, and these
28:809:57982:22:22:71:539:554:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:decisions  depend  on  the  known  exogenous  vectorPBt.   The  equations  are  also  correlated
28:810:58078:22:22:71:539:532:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:through both the permanent unobserved individual heterogeniety (μ) and the time-varying
28:811:58165:22:22:71:539:510:12:g_d0_f3:8:g_d0_f9:unobserved individual heterogeneity (νt), which enter each equation with different effects (ρe
28:812:58259:22:49:71:223:488:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:andωe,e=S,E,M,andK).
28:813:58279:49:29:71:260:439:14:g_d0_f2:14:g_d0_f2:4.3  Body Mass Evolution
28:814:58303:29:22:71:539:410:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:We assume that body mass transitions are captured by a production function that depends
28:815:58390:22:22:71:540:388:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:on caloric intake,ci∗t, and caloric expenditure ,ce∗t:  behaviors chosen by an individual but
28:816:58483:22:34:71:257:366:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:unobserved in our data.  Specifically,
28:817:58521:34:25:117:273:332:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f3:Bt+1=b(Bt,ci∗t,ce∗t,Xt,uBt)
28:818:58548:25:34:150:540:307:12:g_d0_f10:12:g_d0_f3:'φ′fB(Bt,St+1,Et+1,Mt+1,Kt+1,wt,PBt,Xt) +ρBμ+ωBνt+-Bt.(5)
28:819:58605:34:22:72:540:273:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:After substituting in the determinants of caloric intake and expenditure, body mass tran-
28:820:58694:22:22:72:540:251:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:sition  at  the  end  of  the  period  is  a  function  of  the  observed  endogenous  decisions  during
28:821:58798:22:22:72:540:229:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:the period, reflected by the vector of updated (t+ 1) history variables.20Conditional on the
28:822:58890:22:21:72:540:207:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:observed behavior during periodt, only the supply-side variables (PBt) that affect body mass
28:823:58982:21:22:72:539:186:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:inputs affect the body mass transition at the end of periodt.  Note that the unobserved per-
28:824:59074:22:22:72:540:164:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:manent and time-varying individual UH that affects the schooling, employment, marriage,
28:825:59161:22:18:72:484:142:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:and children demand equations also influences body mass at the end of periodt.
28:826:59239:18:16:81:539:124:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:20Theory suggests that non-earned income (including spouse income where marital status is modeled as
28:827:59339:16:12:72:540:108:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:endogenous) and earned income (e.g., wages which are modeled as endogenous) should be included in the
28:828:59440:12:12:72:540:96:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:body mass evolution equation since the determinants of caloric intake and expenditure include one’s income.
28:829:59547:12:12:72:540:84:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:However,  we  found  the  total  household  income  measures  in  the  NLSY  to  be  fairly  noisy  and  frequently
28:830:59662:12:12:72:540:72:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:missing.   Rather  than  include  own  wages  only,  we  have  chosen  to  replace  wages  with  their  reduced  form
28:831:59779:12:0:72:311:60:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:determinants in estimation of the body mass equation.
29:832:59834:0:30:72:264:708:14:g_d0_f2:14:g_d0_f2:4.4  Distribution of Wages
29:833:59860:30:22:72:540:678:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Theory  states  that  individuals  receive  a  wage  offer  each  period  and,  conditional  on  this
29:834:59961:22:22:72:540:656:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:wage, they evaluate the value of each set of alternative behaviors and choose the one that
29:835:60051:22:22:72:540:634:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:maximizes their discounted lifetime utility.  Future wages are uncertain, so individuals must
29:836:60144:22:22:72:540:612:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:evaluate future utility unconditional on future wages (i.e., integrate over the distribution of
29:837:60239:22:22:72:540:590:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:wages).  Empirically, we (the econometricians) do not observe wage offers.  We only observe
29:838:60330:22:22:72:540:568:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:her  accepted  wage,  conditional  on  choosing  employment  in  periodt.   It  is  appropriate  to
29:839:60429:22:22:72:540:546:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:address  the  potential  bias  in  the  marginal  effects  of  explanatory  determinants  of  the  un-
29:840:60531:22:21:72:540:524:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:conditional wage distribution by modeling the UH that impacts selection into employment
29:841:60618:21:22:72:540:503:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:(Equation 2) as well as wages.  According to Mincer’s seminal work, wages are a function of
29:842:60709:22:22:72:540:481:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:observed human capital, typically captured by educational attainment and work experience.
29:843:60798:22:22:72:540:459:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Wages may also vary by productivity differences at the same levels of education and expe-
29:844:60887:22:22:72:540:437:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:rience.  Productivity is generally not observed, and certainly difficult to measure.  We might
29:845:60981:22:22:72:540:415:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:imagine that productivity is influenced by employee characteristics as well as employer or
29:846:61071:22:22:72:539:393:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:job characteristics.21In particular, we allow productivity at work to differ by an employee’s
29:847:61164:22:22:71:539:371:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:marital status and number of children in the household.  Productivity may also be influenced
29:848:61256:22:22:71:539:349:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:by one’s physical health; our only health measure is body mass.  Significance of variation in
29:849:61349:22:22:71:510:327:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:body mass in explaining variation in wages may also reflect preferences by employers.
29:850:61434:22:22:100:540:305:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:For these reasons our periodtwage equation includes variables summarizing the his-
29:851:61516:22:22:72:540:283:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:tory of education,  employment,  marriage,  and children as well as body mass entering the
29:852:61606:22:21:72:540:261:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:current period.  Each of these variables is endogenous, and we address potential bias stem-
29:853:61697:21:22:72:540:240:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:ming from the endogeniety of these wage determinants by modeling the UH that impacts the
29:854:61785:22:22:72:540:218:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:determinants (Equations 1-5) as well as the wages.  We also include as wage determinants
29:855:61873:22:22:72:540:196:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:characteristics  of  the  demand-side  of  the  employment  market  at  the  state  level  (denoted
29:856:61972:22:22:72:540:174:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f3:PEt), aggregate trends (captured by a cubic function of the continuous year variable), and
29:857:62062:22:18:71:539:152:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:geographical region-year interactions to pick up variation in skill prices over locations and
29:858:62155:18:16:81:539:134:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:21We observe an individual’s occupation if she works, but few other employer characteristics.  We could
29:859:62258:16:11:72:540:118:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:model occupational choice and include occupation indicators in the wage equation.  Because this would add
29:860:62363:11:12:72:540:107:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:a large multinomial choice equation and many parameters, we do not jointly model occupation.  We also do
29:861:62467:12:12:72:540:95:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:not include occupation indicators and treat them as exogenous.  Rather, theory tells us that the occupation
29:862:62574:12:12:72:540:83:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:demand  function  is  a  function  of  the  same  variables  that  determine  each  of  the  jointly  chosen  behaviors
29:863:62693:12:12:72:540:71:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:that we do model.  Hence, we assume that the endogenous human capital and productivity histories explain
29:864:62797:12:0:72:122:59:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:occupation.
30:865:62810:0:22:72:540:708:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:time.  If we wished to explain how the mean of the wage distribution varied by its determi-
30:866:62901:22:22:72:540:686:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:nants, we could estimate log wages conditional on being employed as is typical in the labor
30:867:62992:22:22:72:539:664:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:literature.  That is, observed log wages in periodt, conditional on being employed in period
30:868:63084:22:34:72:191:642:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f3:t, would be specified as
30:869:63108:34:34:129:540:608:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:ln(wt|et6= 0) =η′fW(Bt,St,Et,Mt,Kt,Xt,PEt) +ρWμ+ωWνt+-Wt(6)
30:870:63167:34:22:72:539:574:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:where-Wtis  a  serially-uncorrelated  error  term.   Rather  than  use  OLS  to  explain  average
30:871:63264:22:22:72:540:552:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:log  wages,  we  estimate  the  entire  density  of  wages  conditional  on  explanatory  variables
30:872:63363:22:49:72:265:530:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:(explained in the next section below).
30:873:63401:49:29:72:517:481:14:g_d0_f2:14:g_d0_f2:4.5  Conditional Density Estimation of Wages and Body Mass
30:874:63459:29:22:72:540:452:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:As explained above, future wages and body mass are uncertain when individuals are making
30:875:63547:22:22:72:539:430:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:their  periodtdecisions.   Thus,  we  seek  to  estimate  the  marginal  effects  of  theoretically-
30:876:63647:22:22:72:540:408:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:relevant variables on the density of these outcomes rather than simply the first moment, or
30:877:63738:22:22:72:540:386:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:expected value.  To do so, we estimate the density of wages (and of body mass) conditional on
30:878:63831:22:22:72:540:364:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:observable and unobservable variables.  We use the conditional density estimation technique
30:879:63922:22:22:72:540:342:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:(Gilleskie and Mroz, 2004), which allows the effect of an explanatory variable to be different
30:880:64016:22:22:72:417:320:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:at different points of support of the wage (body mass) distribution.
30:881:64084:22:22:100:540:298:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Specifically, we begin by splitting the sample observations intoKintervals.  That is, we
30:882:64172:22:22:72:540:276:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:discretize the support of the dependent variable.  We do this by choosing boundary points
30:883:64261:22:22:72:539:254:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:that  place  an  equal  number  of  observations  in  each  interval  (i.e.,1kth  of  the  sample  falls
30:884:64365:22:22:72:539:232:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:into each bin or interval) such thatwkis the wage that represents thekth percentile of the
30:885:64455:22:21:72:540:210:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:ordered wages.  Figure 4 displays the histogram of wages with cut points associated with
30:886:64543:21:22:72:113:189:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f3:K= 10.
30:887:64549:22:22:100:539:167:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:The probability of an individual’s wage offer being from thekth interval (bounded by
30:888:64633:22:34:72:446:145:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:cutoff wageswk−1andwk), conditional on the explanatory variablesx, is
30:889:64702:34:0:203:320:111:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f3:p[wk−1≤W≤wk|x] =
30:890:64718:0:27:323:345:127:12:g_d0_f19:6:g_d0_f20:∫wk
30:891:64721:27:0:330:350:100:8:g_d0_f9:6:g_d0_f15:wk−1
30:892:64725:0:0:352:408:111:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f8:f(w|x)dw .
31:893:64737:0:34:138:473:431:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Figure 4:  Histogram of Observed Wages with Interval Cut Points
31:894:64800:34:22:72:540:397:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:We can define the discrete time hazard function capturing the probability of being in the
31:895:64889:22:36:72:387:375:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f3:kth interval conditional on not being in a previous interval as
31:896:64952:36:0:136:362:339:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f3:λ(k,x) =p[wk−1≤W≤wk|x,W≥wk−1] =
31:897:64983:0:13:378:396:359:12:g_d0_f19:6:g_d0_f20:∫wk

31:901:65005:14:0:392:466:326:8:g_d0_f9:12:g_d0_f8:w0f(w|x)dw
31:902:65015:0:35:471:474:339:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f8:.
31:903:65016:35:22:72:539:304:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Using  this  hazard  representation,  we  re-write  the  probability  of  a  wage  falling  in  thekth
31:904:65118:22:22:72:125:282:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:interval as
31:905:65129:22:0:186:341:260:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f3:p[wk−1≤W≤wk|x] =λ(k,x)
31:906:65151:0:29:343:358:275:8:g_d0_f9:12:g_d0_f19:k−1∏

31:908:65158:0:33:358:424:260:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f8:[1−λ(j,x)].
31:909:65169:33:22:72:540:227:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:We can recover any moment of the distribution once we have estimated this hazard func-
31:910:65255:22:21:72:540:205:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:tion (which allows us to form the density).  For example,  the expected value of the wage
31:911:65344:21:37:72:290:184:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:conditional on the explanatory variables is
31:912:65387:37:0:194:245:147:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f3:E[W|x] =
31:913:65395:0:30:253:265:162:8:g_d0_f9:12:g_d0_f19:K∑
31:914:65397:30:0:249:265:132:8:g_d0_f9:8:g_d0_f4:k=1
31:915:65400:0:0:267:341:147:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f3:w(k|K)λ(k,x)
31:916:65412:0:29:342:357:162:8:g_d0_f9:12:g_d0_f19:k−1∏
31:917:65416:29:0:342:357:133:8:g_d0_f9:8:g_d0_f4:j=1
31:918:65419:0:39:358:417:147:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:[1−λ(j,x)]
31:919:65429:39:19:72:473:108:12:g_d0_f3:8:g_d0_f4:wherew(k|K) is the mean of the observed wages,w, within thekth interval.22
31:920:65503:19:15:81:539:89:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:22Note that it is important to estimate the employment outcome jointly with the conditional density of
31:921:65605:15:12:72:540:74:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:wages (as we do) in order to account for selection that may bias the estimated marginal effects of explanatory
31:922:65715:12:0:72:404:62:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:variables when we only observe the accepted wages of employed individuals.
32:923:65791:0:22:100:540:708:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:It  remains  to  estimate  the  conditional  hazard,λ(k,x).   First,  we  replicate  each  of
32:924:65884:22:22:72:539:686:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:the person-year observationsK−1 times.  We then define a dependent variable indicating
32:925:65970:22:22:72:539:664:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:whether or not the wage of a particular person in yeartfalls into each of theK−1 intervals.
32:926:66061:22:22:72:540:642:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:(The indicator is undefined for intervals greater than the observed wage.)  We know that the
32:927:66153:22:22:72:540:620:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:unconditional probability of an observed wage being in thekth interval given that it is not
32:928:66244:22:22:72:539:598:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:in an earlier interval is1K−(k−1)based on the way we created the intervals.  Next, we define
32:929:66336:22:22:72:540:576:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:a transformation of the interval counter asik=−ln(K−k) fork < K.  It follows that the
32:930:66421:22:0:71:384:554:12:g_d0_f3:8:g_d0_f9:logit probability for each indicatorik,λ(k,i) =logit(ik) =e
32:931:66480:0:13:384:391:562:6:g_d0_f20:6:g_d0_f20:ik
32:932:66482:13:17:375:540:549:8:g_d0_f4:12:g_d0_f3:1+eik, gives us the unconditional
32:933:66515:17:22:72:540:532:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:probability of the observed wage being in thekth interval (given that it is not in a previous
32:934:66608:22:22:72:540:510:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:interval) exactly because of the way we have defined the interval counter transformation.  To
32:935:66701:22:22:72:539:488:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:estimate the conditional hazard (i.e., conditional on variablesx), we interact the transformed
32:936:66795:22:21:72:437:466:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:interval counter (i.e., the variableik) fully with the wage determinants.
32:937:66868:21:22:100:540:445:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:The hazards for wages and body mass are jointly estimated with other equations in
32:938:66949:22:22:72:540:423:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:our full model (i.e.,  Equations 1-4 and the attrition and initial conditions equations) and
32:939:67041:22:22:72:540:401:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:correlated through the permanent and time-varying UH whose distributions we also estimate.
32:940:67131:22:22:72:540:379:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Once the conditional hazard is estimated we can use it to recover the mean wage and body
32:941:67219:22:22:72:540:357:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:mass conditional on the observables (and unobservables, in our case).  The marginal effects
32:942:67310:22:22:72:540:335:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:of the explanatory variables on the mean wage (body mass) account for differences in effects
32:943:67402:22:49:72:417:313:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:at different points of support of the wage (body mass) distribution.
32:944:67470:49:30:72:402:264:14:g_d0_f2:14:g_d0_f2:4.6  Attrition and Initial Condition Equations
32:945:67516:30:22:72:540:234:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:In order to correctly estimate the distribution of permanent and time-varying UH, we must
32:946:67605:22:21:72:540:212:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:account for the fact that individuals attrit from our research sample over time.  We include,
32:947:67698:21:22:72:540:191:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:in the jointly estimated set of equations, an equation for the probability of attrition at the
32:948:67792:22:22:72:540:169:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:end  of  the  period.   This  probability  depends  on  updated  history  variables,  reflecting  the
32:949:67893:22:22:72:232:147:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:periodtdecisions, and the UH.
32:950:67922:22:22:100:540:125:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Our analysis of individual behavior begins in 1984 when some of the endogenous vari-
32:951:68006:22:22:72:540:103:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:ables are non-zero.  We cannot explain the variation in these observed initial conditions using
32:952:68101:22:22:72:540:81:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:the dynamic specification of behaviors described above because we do not model the history
32:953:68191:22:0:72:540:59:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:of one’s decisions prior to 1984 (i.e.,  we have restricted the model to  explaining behavior
33:954:68286:0:22:72:540:708:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:after  age  18).   For  this  reason  we  specify  reduced-form  equations  that  depend  on  exoge-
33:955:68386:22:22:72:540:686:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:nous  characteristics,  including  variables  that  do  not  influence  the  subsequent  per-period
33:956:68485:22:22:72:540:664:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:outcomes (conditional on the endogenous history variables).  These initial condition equa-
33:957:68575:22:22:72:540:642:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:tions are jointly estimated with the subsequent dynamic demand and outcome equations and
33:958:68663:22:22:72:540:620:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:correlated through the permanent individual UH. The endogenous state variables entering
33:959:68750:22:22:72:540:598:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:the  first  period  (1984),  when  individuals  are  18-26  years  old,  include:  years  of  schooling
33:960:68853:22:22:72:540:576:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:(continuous variable:  7-16), marital state (logit variable:  0,1), years married if married (con-
33:961:68951:22:22:72:540:554:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:tinuous variable:  1-4), years single if single (continuous variable:  1-4), number of children
33:962:69046:22:22:72:540:532:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:(continuous  variable:  0-7),  employment  state  (multinomial  logit  variable:  full-time,  part-
33:963:69145:22:22:72:540:510:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:time, not employed), years of full time experience (continuous variable:  0-5), years of part
33:964:69238:22:22:72:540:488:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:time experience (continuous variable:  0-5), and BMI (continuous variable).  Identification of
33:965:69332:22:21:72:540:466:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:these initial conditions is achieved by inclusion of exogenous time-varying variables (Ptwhen
33:966:69425:21:22:72:540:445:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f3:t=  0) and other individual characteristics (e.g.,  mother’s and father’s years of education
33:967:69517:22:22:72:540:423:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:and indicators of mother’s, father’s, and child’s (at birth and age 14) non-U.S. citizenship).
33:968:69611:22:22:100:539:401:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Additionally,  we  observe  wages  of  those  individuals  who  are  employed  in  periodt.
33:969:69702:22:22:71:539:379:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:However, in the NLSY data, wages are missing for some employed individuals.  In a typical
33:970:69791:22:22:71:539:357:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:year, about 6 percent of wages are missing.  We include an equation to represent any non-
33:971:69880:22:22:71:539:335:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:randomness in missing wages.  In 1995, 1997, 1999, and 2001, however, wages are missing for
33:972:69971:22:22:71:540:313:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:all individuals in the sample because they were not surveyed in these years.23Table 5 below
33:973:70062:22:57:71:449:291:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:summarizes the jointly estimated set of equations and their determinants.
33:974:70135:57:44:71:250:234:17:g_d0_f2:17:g_d0_f2:5  Empirical Results
33:975:70155:44:30:71:350:190:14:g_d0_f2:14:g_d0_f2:5.1  Replication of Previous Literature
33:976:70194:30:22:71:539:160:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:In order to convince the reader that our preferred model addresses omissions in the literature
33:977:70288:22:19:71:539:138:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:(namely dynamics and UH bias correction), we first estimate models similar to those in the
33:978:70378:19:15:81:539:119:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:23We are able to construct values for demand behaviors during these non-surveyed years given observed
33:979:70479:15:12:72:540:104:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:behaviors in surveyed years.  For example, while we do not observe whether someone was married in 1995,
33:980:70582:12:12:72:540:92:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:we do observe their marital status in 1994 and 1996.  We make assumptions during years with missing data
33:981:70686:12:12:72:540:80:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:for only a small number of person-years.  If the assumptions required to fill in these missing years are too
33:982:70794:12:0:72:322:68:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:strict we delete that individual from our research sample.

34:1001:71668:22:22:72:540:330:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:3).  That is, Model 1 is a very sparse model of log wages on BMI and several demographic
34:1002:71756:22:21:72:540:308:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:variables.  We find that a contemporaneous reduction in body mass from overweight status
34:1003:71844:21:22:72:540:287:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:(BMI = 27.5) to normal weight status (BMI = 24) results in a $0.31 increase in hourly wages
34:1004:71935:22:22:72:540:265:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:for white women and a $0.12 increase in the hourly wages of black women, where increases
34:1005:72023:22:22:72:540:243:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:for  black  women  are  statistically  significant.24Put  differently,  a  five  percent  decrease  in
34:1006:72125:22:22:72:540:221:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:BMI (i.e.,  equivalent to about a seven pound annual weight loss for a 5’ 4” female) leads
34:1007:72215:22:22:72:540:199:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:to an $0.11 increase in hourly wages for white females and a $0.02 increase in hourly wages
34:1008:72306:22:22:72:540:177:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:for  black  females.25When  we  include  (in  Model  2)  variables  representing  human  capital
34:1009:72402:22:22:72:540:155:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:(but  treat  them  as  exogenous),  the  marginal  effect  of  moving  from  overweight  to  normal
34:1010:72501:22:18:72:540:133:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:weight is a $0.27 and $0.14 increase in wages, respectively.  (A five percent decrease in BMI
34:1011:72594:18:16:81:539:115:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:24If we do not control for deviations from the mean BMI by race, BMI impacts on wages are statistically
34:1012:72697:16:8:72:458:99:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:significant for both races (as the literature reports with this simple biased specification).
34:1013:72790:8:16:81:539:91:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:25To  report  marginal  effects  in  level  dollars  we  transform  from  logs  to  levels  assuming  normality  and
34:1014:72906:16:0:72:250:75:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:homoskedasticity of the wage error term.
35:1015:72948:0:22:72:540:708:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:results in an estimated $0.09 and $0.04 increase, respectively.)  When we control for demand
35:1016:73040:22:22:72:540:686:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:side wage shifters (Model 3) the marginal effect of body mass changes very little.  In the
35:1017:73130:22:22:72:540:664:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:latter two cases, the impact of body mass on the wages of white and black women are less
35:1018:73218:22:22:72:540:642:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:precisely estimated.  Model 4 of Table 6 presents the estimates and marginal effects when
35:1019:73307:22:22:72:540:620:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:the model is estimated with individual fixed effects.  These fixed effects purge the coefficient
35:1020:73403:22:22:72:540:598:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:estimates of bias associated with permanent individual UH that might be correlated with
35:1021:73490:22:22:72:540:576:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:wages and body mass (as well as education, work experience, marriage, and children).  The
35:1022:73579:22:22:72:540:554:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:resulting marginal effect of body mass on wages of white women is much smaller ($0.07) and
35:1023:73669:22:22:72:540:532:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:is significantly estimated at the one percent level.  The effect of a reduction in weight on
35:1024:73761:22:22:72:540:510:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:wages of black women becomes negative, costing $0.05 (for a reduction from overweight to
35:1025:73849:22:22:72:539:488:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:normal weight).26We find that the large reduction in impact after controlling for permanent
35:1026:73940:22:21:71:539:466:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:heterogeneity is consistent with the literature.  However, the results after introduction of a
35:1027:74034:21:22:71:539:445:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:variable to capture deviation from the average BMI of individuals of one’s same race suggest
35:1028:74126:22:22:71:348:423:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:that these smaller impacts are statistically significant.
35:1029:74183:22:22:100:540:401:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:We also calculate the marginal effects using a quantile regression (QR) estimator in or-
35:1030:74271:22:22:71:539:379:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:der to determine whether the marginal effect of a change in BMI is different at different levels
35:1031:74367:22:22:71:540:357:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:of wages, as was examined by Johar and Katayama (2012).27Displayed in Table 7, we find
35:1032:74453:22:22:71:539:335:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:that a reduction from overweight to normal weight among females leads to a smaller average
35:1033:74543:22:22:71:539:313:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:increase in hourly wages in the bottom quartile of the distribution than in higher quartiles.
35:1034:74636:22:22:71:539:291:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:On average, the wage increases are $0.26 and $0.15 for white and black women, respectively.
35:1035:74727:22:22:71:539:269:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:These  findings  are  qualitatively  similar  to  those  of  Johar  and  Katayama;  they  are  larger
35:1036:74828:22:22:71:539:247:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:because we do not account for the endogeneity of BMI in these exploratory replications with
35:1037:74919:22:22:71:539:225:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:our data.  The conditional density estimation (CDE) approach is more general and flexible
35:1038:75008:22:21:71:539:203:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:than the QR approach, and is easier to use in more complicated models that involve correc-
35:1039:75098:21:22:71:539:182:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:tion for selection (into employment) and endogeneity (of relevant explanatory variables such
35:1040:75190:22:22:71:539:160:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:as BMI, education, experience, and productivity proxies).  Estimating a single equation for
35:1041:75281:22:18:71:539:138:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:wages using the CDE approach, we find average wage effects of the over- to normal-weight
35:1042:75369:18:16:81:539:120:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:26A five percent decrease in weight leads to an increase in wages of white women on $0.02 and a decrease
35:1043:75473:16:8:72:252:104:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:in wages of black women of almost $0.04.
35:1044:75513:8:16:81:539:96:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:27These authors account for the endogeneity of BMI using an IV approach.  They control for, but treat
35:1045:75614:16:12:72:540:80:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:as exogenous, educational attainment, type of employment, work experience, marital status, and number of
35:1046:75718:12:0:72:380:68:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:biological children.  They do not control for selection into employment.

38:1127:78576:0:34:72:350:708:14:g_d0_f2:14:g_d0_f2:5.2  Results from our Preferred Model
38:1128:78613:34:30:72:445:674:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:Graphical Fit of Model’s Ability to Capture Dynamic Behavior
38:1129:78673:30:22:72:540:644:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Appendix Tables A1-A6 present coefficient estimates from our dynamic multiple equation
38:1130:78759:22:22:72:540:622:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:model  that  accounts  for  both  permanent  and  time-varying  individual  UH  and  allows  for
38:1131:78855:22:22:72:540:600:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:different effects across the distributions of body mass and wages.28We begin by depicting, in
38:1132:78948:22:21:72:540:578:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Figure 6 and 7, outcomes from our data generating process (i.e., the jointly-estimated wage,
38:1133:79040:21:22:72:540:557:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:demand, and production equations) with the observed data.  We simulate behavior from the
38:1134:79128:22:22:72:540:535:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:first period forward, using the simulated values of endogenous explanatory variables updated
38:1135:79220:22:22:72:540:513:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:each period based on the estimated dynamic equations.  The updated simulations from the
38:1136:79307:22:22:72:540:491:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:model with heterogeneity suggest that the model captures well the dynamics associated with
38:1137:79397:22:43:72:198:469:12:g_d0_f3:8:g_d0_f4:the modeled behaviors.29
38:1138:79421:43:30:71:271:426:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:Calculation of Model Implications
38:1139:79454:30:21:71:539:396:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Examination of the coefficient estimates is not sufficient for understanding the dynamic role
38:1140:79547:21:22:71:539:375:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:of body mass on wages over time.  In order to calculate the contemporaneous and dynamic
38:1141:79634:22:22:71:539:353:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:marginal effects of variables of interest on outcomes of interest, we simulate behavior over
38:1142:79726:22:22:71:539:331:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:time  using  the  estimated  parameters  on  observed  variables,  the  estimated  distribution  of
38:1143:79825:22:22:71:539:309:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:UH, and assumed distributions of the remaining i.i.d unobservables.  For example, we might
38:1144:79915:22:22:71:539:287:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:desire to know how an improvement in body mass affects wages.  But,  is that a one time
38:1145:80002:22:22:71:539:265:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:decrease  in  body  mass  at  age  18?   Or  a  decrease  each  year?   And  do  we  care  about  the
38:1146:80103:22:22:71:539:243:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:average effect on wages over the life cycle, or at age 18, or at age 40?  Any change in body
38:1147:80195:22:18:71:470:221:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:mass at a particular age will have an effect on wages at every subsequent age.
38:1148:80273:18:15:81:539:203:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:28Estimates for the initial conditions equations are available from the authors.  We have verified that the
38:1149:80380:15:12:72:540:188:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:variables we include in the initial condition equations as identifying variables are statistically significant and
38:1150:80494:12:12:72:540:176:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:can be excluded from the main equations conditional on the inclusion of the endogenous variables.  Similarly,
38:1151:80603:12:12:72:540:164:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:to test our exclusions restrictions in the dynamic equations, we have estimated a model where we include
38:1152:80707:12:12:72:540:152:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:variables in equations for which theory suggests they should be excluded.  Tests of statistical significance
38:1153:80815:12:12:72:540:140:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:confirm that these identifying variables can be excluded after conditioning on the endogenous variables that
38:1154:80923:12:8:72:142:128:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:they do explain.
38:1155:80939:8:16:81:539:120:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:29Likelihood ratio tests suggest that a model with six estimated discrete mass points for the permanent UH
38:1156:81045:16:12:72:540:104:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:distribution [0.00 (0.02), 0.27 (0.10), 0.45 (0.19), 0.61 (0.34), 0.79 (0.27), 1.00, (0.08)] and three estimated
38:1157:81157:12:12:72:540:92:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:discrete mass points for the time-varying UH distribution [0.00 (0.08), 0.60 (0.92), 1.00 (0.01)] provides the
38:1158:81267:12:0:72:453:80:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:best statistical fit.  Mass point values are listed with probability weights in parentheses.

40:1160:81422:0:0:147:465:218:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Figure 6:  Comparison of Observed Data to Model Predictions
41:1161:81483:0:22:100:540:708:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Since our goal is to understand the dynamic role of body mass on wages, we focus our
41:1162:81567:22:22:72:540:686:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:attention here on understanding these marginal effects, and we encourage the reader to take
41:1163:81658:22:22:72:540:664:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:note of the statistical significance of estimated parameters (available in Tables A1-A6).  Note
41:1164:81753:22:22:72:540:642:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:that we report impacts on both the conditional (on employment) and unconditional wage
41:1165:81838:22:22:72:540:620:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:distribution (while also conditioning on observed wage determinants).  First,  we calculate
41:1166:81929:22:22:72:540:598:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:the effect of an improvement in body mass from overweight (BMI=27.5) to normal weight
41:1167:82014:22:22:72:540:576:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:(BMI=24) on contemporaneous wages.  That is,  if one’s body mass currently was normal
41:1168:82099:22:22:72:540:554:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:weight rather than overweight (all else equal), how might the hourly wage distribution differ?
41:1169:82193:22:22:72:539:532:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:We call this thecontemporaneous  effect.  This effect does not include the effect on human
41:1170:82283:22:22:72:540:510:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:capital  formation  and  accumulation  (which  also  affect  wages  this  year)  of  the  changes  in
41:1171:82384:22:22:72:540:488:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:evolutionary  body  mass  that  may  have  precipitated  the  current  change.   Figure  8  plots
41:1172:82481:22:21:72:540:466:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:the estimated wage differences across deciles for our preferred model with UH (solid lines),
41:1173:82573:21:22:72:540:445:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:which addresses endogeneity, selection, and measurement error; it also displays the associated
41:1174:82667:22:22:72:540:423:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:results for a model without UH (dotted lines), which represents biased impacts.  The no UH
41:1175:82757:22:22:72:540:401:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:estimates  reflect  gains  similar  to  those  described  previously  by  our  replications  of  models
41:1176:82860:22:22:72:540:379:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:used  in  the  literature.   Our  preferred  model  that  includes  UH  suggests  that,  on  average,
41:1177:82961:22:22:72:540:357:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:the unbiased contemporaneous wage impacts are smaller; white women experience a $0.07
41:1178:83046:22:22:72:540:335:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:increase  in  hourly  wages  and  black  women  experience  a  small  decrease  of  $0.02.   These
41:1179:83144:22:22:72:540:313:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:averages reflect increases in wages at all deciles for whites while blacks experience reductions
41:1180:83240:22:22:72:460:291:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:in wages that fall in the first six deciles, but improvements at higher wages.
41:1181:83318:22:22:100:540:269:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:We can compare the contemporaneous effect of body mass on wages with that of human
41:1182:83400:22:22:72:540:247:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:capital and productivity variables.  One year increases in education lead to contemporaneous
41:1183:83492:22:22:72:540:225:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:increases in hourly wages of $0.79 and $0.66 for white and black females, respectively.  One
41:1184:83584:22:21:72:540:203:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:additional  year  of  full  time  employment  experience  leads  to  increases  of  $0.26  and  $0.21,
41:1185:83686:21:22:72:540:182:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:respectively.  The associated reduction of body mass by one BMI point leads to increases in
41:1186:83777:22:22:72:540:160:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:contemporaneous wages of $0.05 for white women and decreases of $0.01 for black women,
41:1187:83863:22:22:72:130:138:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:on average.
41:1188:83874:22:22:100:539:116:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Now we want to calculate thedynamic effect(or life-cycle effect) of an improvement in
41:1189:83959:22:0:72:540:94:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:body mass from overweight to normal weight.  That is, we simulate schooling, employment,
42:1190:84049:0:0:118:493:218:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Figure 7:  Contemporaneous Effect of Body Mass Improvement on Wages
43:1191:84118:0:22:72:540:708:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:marriage, and family size behaviors of individuals if they were normal or overweight through-
43:1192:84211:22:22:72:540:686:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:out their lives (ages 18 through 45) and compare the predicted wages for each body mass
43:1193:84298:22:22:72:540:664:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:type each period.  Note that this calculation includes both the change in contemporaneous
43:1194:84387:22:22:72:540:642:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:wages (via body mass) as well as the changes in wages caused by the effects of body mass on
43:1195:84478:22:22:72:540:620:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:human capital (via education and experience) and productivity (via marriage and children).
43:1196:84568:22:22:100:540:598:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Figure 9 depicts the average differences in wages associated with an improvement in

43:1199:84839:22:22:72:540:532:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:is an average increase in hourly wages of $0.04.  Black females experience a decline of $0.07
43:1200:84932:22:22:72:540:510:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:on  average.   Recall  that  the  contemporaneous  effects  averaged  $0.07  and  $-0.02  for  white
43:1201:85032:22:22:72:540:488:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:and black females, respectively.  These results suggest that the permanent reduction in body
43:1202:85124:22:21:72:540:466:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:mass (from overweight to normal weight) led to changes in human capital and productivity
43:1203:85212:21:22:72:540:445:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:behaviors over the life cycle that generated additional contributions to wages that reflect a
43:1204:85305:22:22:72:540:423:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:smaller total life-cycle body mass impact.  In general, a weight improvement results in the
43:1205:85396:22:22:72:540:401:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:greatest nominal wage increase for white and black women in the highest deciles of wages.
43:1206:85485:22:22:72:540:379:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:However, black women are likely to experience a wage reduction in the wage deciles where
43:1207:85573:22:22:72:256:357:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:they are more likely to be observed.
43:1208:85609:22:22:100:540:335:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:For comparison, the life-cycle impact of a one year increase in schooling is $1.16 and

43:1210:85797:22:22:72:540:291:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:wages.  A one year increase in full-time work experience increases wages by $0.40 and $0.41
43:1211:85888:22:22:72:540:269:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:for white and black women.  The life-cycle effect of aone unitdecrease in BMI on wages, on

46:1234:87703:22:22:100:540:111:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:To quantify these distributional impacts of wages across the life cycle, we examine the

47:1236:87881:0:35:115:499:564:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:a.)  Unconditional on Employmentb.)  Conditional on Being Employed
47:1237:87947:35:34:120:490:529:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Figure 11:  Effect of Body Mass Improvement on Average Wages by Age
47:1238:88014:34:22:72:539:495:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:40.30First,  for each individual in the estimation sample replicated 50 times,  we simulate
47:1239:88105:22:22:72:540:473:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:their behavior each year regarding education, employment, marriage, and children, as well
47:1240:88194:22:22:72:540:451:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:as  body  mass  and  wages.   We  call  this  our  baseline,  which  represents  the  wage  outcomes
47:1241:88294:22:22:72:540:429:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:associated with endogenous behaviors and body mass evolution over the life cycle.  We then
47:1242:88384:22:22:72:540:407:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:force a body mass trajectory on each replicated individual.  For example, we may force the
47:1243:88474:22:22:72:540:385:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:individual to be normal weight over the age range (e.g., age 18 to 45).  Table 10 describes
47:1244:88565:22:22:72:540:363:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:the differences in average wages and total earnings over the ages of 25 through 40 for each
47:1245:88656:22:22:72:271:341:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:of the imposed body mass trajectories.
47:1246:88694:22:22:100:540:319:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:In the baseline model simulation, body mass and behaviors are determined by the model
47:1247:88779:22:22:72:540:297:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:estimates endogenously and updated each period.  Thus, body mass is allowed to fluctuate as
47:1248:88870:22:22:72:540:275:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:individuals age.  In the simulations that follow, we force body weight to be a specified level
47:1249:88964:22:21:72:540:253:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:each period, but continue to let behaviors and wages be determined endogenously according
47:1250:89053:21:22:72:540:232:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:to the dynamic model.  The average of the sum of wage draw differences when body mass
47:1251:89138:22:22:72:540:210:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:is normal (BMI=24) at age 25 through age 40 relative to the baseline (when body mass is
47:1252:89225:22:19:72:540:188:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:freely determined at each age) is negative.31However, what is relevant for our analysis is the
47:1253:89319:19:15:81:539:169:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:30Ostbye, et al.  (2011) use the NLSY79 data to classify individuals into groups characterized by their BMI
47:1254:89426:15:12:72:540:154:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:trajectory.  These epidemiologists identify four trajectory groups:  normal weight, overweight, late adulthood
47:1255:89536:12:12:72:540:142:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:obesity, and early adulthood obesity.  Grouping individuals together by gender, race, and cohort, they report
47:1256:89645:12:12:72:540:130:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:membership rates of 35.0, 41.2, 19.7, and 4.2 percent for the four trajectory groupings, respectively.  Males,
47:1257:89755:12:12:72:540:118:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:blacks  and  those  in  the  younger  age  cohort  (aged  18  to  20  years  in  1981)  were  more  likely  to  be  in  the
47:1258:89878:12:9:72:526:106:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:overweight, late overweight, and early overweight trajectory groups relative to the normal weight group.
47:1259:89982:9:15:81:539:97:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:31Note  that  the  wage  values  in  these  calculations  are  draws  from  the  wage  distribution  conditional  on
47:1260:90098:15:12:72:540:82:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:an individual’s histories of human and health capital, regardless of employment decision (column one).  In
47:1261:90204:12:0:72:458:70:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:column two we average the sum of the wage differences conditional on choosing to work.
49:1262:91584:0:22:72:540:708:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:size of this difference when body mass is simulated to be overweight (BMI = 27.5) or obese
49:1263:91674:22:22:72:540:686:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:(BMI = 32) relative to that difference when body mass is normal.  For white females, the
49:1264:91762:22:22:72:540:664:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:loss in hourly wages grows as body mass increases.  Translated into total earnings over ages

49:1276:92785:22:22:72:540:401:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:associated with body mass can reach as high as two percent of total earnings over this age
49:1277:92875:22:22:72:103:379:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:range.

49:1282:93239:22:22:72:540:269:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:overweight (row four) and black.  Obesity among black women who choose to work amounts
49:1283:93325:22:22:72:540:247:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:to  a  $170  loss  in  total  earnings  when  she  chooses  to  be  employed  (row  eight).   The  wage
49:1284:93428:22:22:72:540:225:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:penalties (or gains) for black women are tiny relative to total earnings over this age range.
49:1285:93521:22:21:72:540:203:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Recall, however, that these figures represent averages over the entire wage distribution.  The
49:1286:93615:21:22:72:540:182:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:wage penalties at the high end of the wage distribution are large and significant for both
49:1287:93705:22:0:72:196:160:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:white and black women.
50:1288:93729:0:34:72:194:708:17:g_d0_f2:17:g_d0_f2:6  Conclusion
50:1289:93742:34:22:72:540:674:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:We find significant differences in the contemporaneous effect and the dynamic effect of body
50:1290:93834:22:22:72:540:652:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:mass  on  wages,  both  across  females  of  different  races  and  over  the  distribution  of  wages.
50:1291:93937:22:22:72:540:630:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:We highlight several differences between our model and those models frequently used in this
50:1292:94028:22:22:72:540:608:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:literature:  we use a richer incorporation of body mass, we account for selection into employ-
50:1293:94122:22:22:72:540:586:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:ment, we model endogenous body mass transitions, we jointly model endogenous variables
50:1294:94208:22:22:72:540:564:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:(related to the history of schooling, employment, marriage, and children) that impact wages
50:1295:94299:22:22:72:540:542:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:over time, we use random effects vs fixed effects to capture permanent heterogeneity which
50:1296:94389:22:22:72:540:520:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:allows  us  to  retain  non-time-varying  wage  determinants,  we  include  both  permanent  and
50:1297:94485:22:21:72:540:498:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:time-varying individual unobserved heterogeneity, and we estimate the impact of determi-
50:1298:94573:21:22:72:540:477:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:nants  on  the  density  of  wages  and  body  mass  rather  than  simply  the  mean.   Our  model
50:1299:94671:22:22:72:540:455:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:enables us to disentangle these two effects of interest.  It also allows us to understand the
50:1300:94764:22:0:72:412:433:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:mechanisms over the life cycle that contribute to these differences.
